One of Mr. Herrin's chief ?ources of delight is to dwell on the fact that lawyers

One cannot say that Mr. Herri recounts to men his achievements as a com
mar; of lawyers, but the subject is frequently Introduced to charm the angels

If Passenger Agent McCormick enters the political arena in California he
may be sure of going in under what is commonly called the most favorable aus
pices. No matter what he may dt> he â will not do worse than Herrin has done.
In Ohio, Illinois and Indiana Mr. McCormick looked after the Interests of the
"Big Four." In the four big cities which the rails join Mr. McCormick was
familiar with the local politics of the several towns. He knew Mayors and
members of Congress and was on intimate speaking terms with several Gover
nors, but with none just like ours. He came West, like many of his predecess
ors, to enlarge his horizon and his salary, but the change of base has not dimin
ished or dulled his fancy for taking a hand In politics.
.v doubt there is a fine field in California for a railroad man who possesses
the double qualification of a passenger traffic manager and political manipulator.
A passenger man like Mr. McCormick ought to know how to place passes where
they will do the most good. Herrin's first blunder In the late Senatorial cam
paign was committed with the assistance of a pass, but as the campaign pro
gressed he got so that he could commit a blunder without any assistance.

members.
It is remarked to Mr. McCormick' s credit that he conducts political affairs

A NEW BALLOON TO WASTE HIS BREATH ON.
BOTH BRITONS AND BOERS CONTINUE
PREPARATIONS FOR WAR.
LONDON, Sept. 20.â Despite all
outward show of calm. Great
Britain is in practically the same
condition to-day a^ \ras >he
T'nited States a few wwks before
the opening of the war with Spain. Be
neath the crust of diplomatic reserve
the military officials are working night
and day preparing for the signal to be
gin hostilities, whether that comes or
not.
England is not going to be caught
napping any more than the Transvaal.
While Lord Salisbury is quietly at
home in Hatfteld House. Mr. chamber
lain is buried in work at the Colonial
Office, and double forces at the Royal
Arsenal and dockyards are straining
every nerve to equip and transport
troops to the Cape.
It may be set down for certain that
Great Britain will do nothing to pre
cipitate matters, and the Colonial
Office lfi far from admitting that the
case is hopeless.
The rer.ort that Great Britain had
demanded the dismantlement of tho
fort at Johannesburg, together with a
material reduction in the armament of
the burghers, the Colonial Office to-day
would neither confirm nor deny, al
though it is much doubted.
Meanwhile the Marquis of Lans
downe, Secretary of State for War
who returned this morning from Dub
lin, has held long conferences with
General Lord Garnet Wolseley, field
marshal, and General Sir Evelyn
Wood, adjutant general of the forces,
and the activity at headquarters is re
flected in the contract, supply and
transport departments.

port Jeliunge for the Mediterranean to

At Woolwich activity increases daily,

While rumors of the reassembling of
Parliament are current, the fact is re
called that in the Afghan war in IS7S
Great Britain declared war first and
then assembled Parliament.
This evening the Marquis of Lans
downe issued a strict order to the staff
of the War Office warning all subordi
nates to refrain from giving the press
any information whatever without the
authority of their superiors, and call

Advices . from Cape Town are to the

and that if the imperial Government
does not recede from its present post
tlon war is inevitable. The South Afri
can News supports the contention of

ISS4 abolished the suzerainty.
According to other advices the burgh
ers are rapidly going into laager, while
the exodus from Johannesburg reached
1000 persons yesterday. The Stock Ex
change at Johannesburg has resolved
to close the moment martial law is pro
claimed, but all current contracts will
be carried out until the proclamation
suspending all business. In the mean
time the merchants are erecting barri
cades In fmnt of their shop windows.
At Pretoria the interest seems to be
centered in the meeting of the Orange
Free State Volksraad to-morrow, which
If is hoped may suggest a modus vi
vendi that would save the situation.
<Â»n the other hand, advices from

there of a large number of members of

as the Orange Free State will throw in

State burghers being fully armed and
ready to start at a moment's notice.
A special dispatch from Johannes
burg says that 450 persons left by one
train to-day.

ASSESSOR DODGE IN
A VERY BAD LIGHT
Fails to Account for Taxes on
Nine Millions of Property,
Auditor Wells' Demand Upon Hii lor the
Money Is Ignoredâ Matter in the
Hands of the District
Attorney.
ASSESSOR DODGE may soon be forced, if the law takes Its course, to prove
that all the nice things he has been saying about himself are not the
veriest .cheap political claptrap. There is now in the hands of District
Attorney Murphy for legal action the City Auditor's demand upon the
Assessor for the sum of $122.982 04, which Dr. Dodge in his official capacity is in
duty bound, to cover into the city treasury. He has been requested so to do
| by Auditor Wells, but has made no response.
; Dr. Dodge has allowed the impression to gain ground that he is "the whole
thing" in the line of Assessors. Like Katlsha's tooth, he stands alone. Before
he was elected he did a juggling act with a piece of chalk, a blackboard and
an assorted lot of figures that would have made Herrman's head swim. He be
gan then to say nice things about himself. He allowed his friend and political
sponsor. Mayor Phelan. to say nice things about him. Both have continued un
' interruptedly to say lots of nice things about the doctor and to allow the
Mayor's personal and partial organ to repeat them. They even threw In a few
figures by way of corroborative detail, on the assumption that figures won't lie
and that figures were such prosaic things that nobody would take the trouble
to verify them. But, with all the figures, with the demand of the Auditor for
that $122.982 04, Assessor Dodge has not turned that sum into the city treasury,
and the fact remains that until he does he is not the lily white Assessor he
would have the public believe.
Last week Assessor Dodge said some more nice things concerning himself.
They were contained in a supplement to his regular report and filed in the
Clerk's office. In it is the following passage, which, one familiar with the work
ings of the office would think, would have refreshed his memory about that
$122,982 04, although he carefully avoids all mention of it:
ThÂ» total assessment roll for the year 189?, after making the corrections and deduc
tions authorized by your honorable body, amounts to 111.615, as against $332,344,061 for
the year 189*. or an increase of $. r 2. "57,554 over the assessment of last year. As the total
assessment of real estate is $344. less this year than the assessment of real estate for
the year IÂ«9S it follows that the marked Increase in the assessment roll has been obtained
through the Increased assessment of personal property.
"Personal property!" That's just where the rub comes in on Assessor Dodge.
He says: "It follows that the marked increase in the assessment roll has been
obtained through the increased assessment of personal property." That is the
first statement that proves, in connection with Auditor Wells' demand on Asses
sor Dodge for $122,952 04, that the Assessor is all right on a blackboard and all
wrong in the office. It opens the way to show that, while Dodge has been all
along patting himself on the back for the good thing he says he is. he is far and
away behind his predecessors in the diligent conduct of his office. In other words,
it makes plain that, while he is the very deuce when It comes to assessing, ho
is only a two-spot on collections. <
The sum of $122,982 04 in question represents the tax on personal property of
the value of 59,330,953 which Dodge assessed all right, but which, it seems, he has
failed to collect. It will be noticed In passing that it is on the Increased assess
ment of personal property that the phenomenal Assessor prides himself. When.
one stops to consider that, notwithstanding this herculean effort the city treasury
is shy just $122,982 04, it is plain oa the face of it that figures won't lie even for
"Juggler" Dodga' and 1122,982 04 is facing him for explanation. , .. ___
The law that demands this explanation is very plain and under it Auditor
Wells has acted. It reads:
Within fifteen days after the first Monday in August of each year the Auditor of the
county, or city and county,^must make a careful examination of the assessment book or
' books of the county, or city and county, and ascertain therefrom the amount or amounts
of all tax?* that should have been collected by th** Assessor in pursuance of this chap
ter, and wbfeh have not been collected. He must then state an account to the Assessor
. Â«nd demand from him that the amount or amounts so remaining uncollected shall be paid
Into the county treasury within fifteen days from the date of said demand. If at the
expiration of said time the Assessor has not settled for and paid said amount or amounts
into the treasury as aforesaid the District Attorney must commence an action in the proper
court. against the Assessor and. his bondsmen for the recovery of said amount or amounts
so remaining uncollected; and upon the trial of said action- no defense shall be ad
missible except that the assessment or assessments are illegal, invalid or void.
That law has been on the statute books for many a day and some of Dodge's
predecessors have failed to account for sums demanded to be accounted for
under it, but .none tof them have been so lax, have left such a large sum un
covered into the treasury as the model Dodge. Here is the record of three years
taken from the books in the Auditor's officeâ and Dodge says figures won't
lie: -_ . - .
In 1537 Assessor Siebe -failed to account for the taxes, amounting to J5652 63, on personal
property assessed at $686,
In 1898 Assessor Siebe failed to account for the taxes, amounting to 13210 S3, on personal
property assessed at $270,573.
In 1899 Assessor Dodge has failed to account for $122,982 01 taxes
on. personal property amounting to $9,330,959.
How does he explain these figures? Will he tell the people that he was so
busy putting into his own pocket the 15 per c*nt of all the poll tax collected that
he didn't have time to look after the city's taxes on unsecured personal property?
Will he explain to the 44.913 voters in San Francisco, many of them poor men,
that both his hands were so deep in their pockets for the 30 cents he got every
time he gave one of them a poll-tax receipt that he had to let the city's busi
ness slide?
Come to think of it. he will not have to tell them. He is authority for the
statement that figures won't He, and there they are for him. The lowly figures
30 will represent the amount of cents he has taken this year from the pocket of
every man in San Francisco he has hounded for poll tax, while he let slide in
his greed for gain a sum twenty thousand dollars in excess of the sum total of
poll tax.
According to the Auditor's books, to date Dodge has collected poll tax from
44.913 persons, representing a total of $*9,526. His commission, dug out of the
pockets of the poor, was $13,473 SO. Since tha,t entry he has collected other poll
tax, but not to exceed $100,000 in all.
The amount he has failed to collect on personal property is $122,982 04.
That is the Dodge idea of assessing.but it is not the law's. It puts money
into Dodge's pocket, but it leaves bare boards jr. the city's chest. That's the
sort of assessing that points to' a "dummy assessment roll," and says "I done it,"
while poor school teachers have to live on bread and water for lack of pay. It is
the kind of assessing that has swelled the assessment roll to $405,000,000, and
says: "My predecessors ain't in it." And it is also the kind of assessing that
says immediately after: "There is $31,000,000 on which I can't collect taxes. They're
on the assessment roll, though, thanks to W. Podge."
He forgot to count in the more than nine millions to which the District At
torney is in duty bound to call his attention. Take these millions off the roll, and
where is Dodge?
It is now the duty of the District Attorney to find out. Under the law he
should file suit immediately against the goody-goody Assessor and compel him
to give an account of his stewardship of nine millions that strayed while Dr.
Dodge was diving Into the pockets of the citizens for his poll-tax perquisite. It
is up to the District Attorney.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 21, 1899.
EARTHQUAKES OVERTURN
MOUNTAINS IN ALASKA
Intermittent Shocks Felt for a
Week Between Bituya Bay
and Yakutat.
By HAL HOFFMAN.
JUNEAU, Alaska, Sept. 14, via Seat
tle, Wash., Sept. 20.â The Alaska Com
mercial Company's steamer, Dora, Cap
tain Johansen, arrived from the west
ward last night with news of an exten
sive and terrible earthquake extending
from Bituya Bay, 150 miles below Ya
kutat, 500 miles northwest into the
Cook Inlet country. It was the great
est phenomenon witnessed in Alaska
since a similar occurrence in the early
days of the Russian traders.
After a short period of darkness the
earth literally sank down into the sea
and new islands and peninsulas rose up
like huge leviathans showing their
backs above the surface. Mountains
were shaken down and their tops slid
into the ocean. The whole was ac
companied by several tidal waves, one
of them fifteen feet high. The shocks
occurred every day for a week, from
Sunday, the 3d inst., to Sunday, the
10th inst., and were felt last Sunday
at Juneau.
Three very hard shocks were felt
here. One early in the morning and
another about 2 o'clock in the after
noon and the third about 5 o'clock in
the afternoon. Buildings were badly
shaken and people ran out into the
streets in their fright. There were
minor tremblings all day long. The
large buildings, such as the Occidental
Hotel, Stannis Hospital and the
churches, shook and rattled like a crazy
ship in a gale. Xo damage to property
was done here or elsewhere. The moun
tains are all in place around Jttceau.
The shocks here continued steadily
from thirty seconds to two minutes. A
gale was blowing at the time. The
eurthquake seemed to be traveling
south and taking plenty of time to
shake up the country as it passed
along.
The most severe shocks were- folt at
Yakutat and Kayak. At Yakutat there
is a large Indian village and several
traders' stores. It is headquarter? tor
minors prospecting in that section of
Alaska and up the Alsek River. Around i
the point of Ocean Cape, at the en- I
trance t" Yakutat Bay, is an island i
named Kanak. This island sank twen- '
ty feet into the sea. At hi.erh tide only
the tops of the trees are visible. Huge
Spanish war without certifications from
the Civil Service Commission.

It is evident from the foregoing dis
patch that General Castro has a for
midable army to attack President An
drade's troops. His action in' evacuat
ing Puerto Cabello after occupying it
and permitting the Venezuelan troops
to take possession is rather puzzling to
the officials in view of the fact that the
port Is quite valuable, both as a ba^se
and as a port through which arms and
ammunition can be obtained. It is pos
sible that Castro thinks it necessary
fissures opened in the earth of the
mainland and steam is said to have
poured forth. Some of these fissures
were three feet across. Captain Johan
sen says that in two minutes the ocean
rose twenty feet above high tide mark
and almost as quickly subsided.
The natives took to the high ground
in terror, and fell on their knees be
seeching the great Father to save them
and their village. This high water
caught up all manner of drift wood,
logs, canoes, etc., and these raced back
and forth and across the bay carried by
strong currents which swept in every
direction. When the Dora arrived at
Yakutat a few days ago on the return
trip the Indians had deserted their
houses near the beach, and were living
in tents on the high ground. They were
still in great alarm.
At least thirty Yakutat Indians
are now in Juneau. having deserted
their homes and all their property fn
seeking safety in flight.
A good story is told on the two Swed
ish missionaries at Yakutat, Rev. Mr.
Johnson and Rev. Mr. Hendrickson. They
wanted to leave, but the Indians would
not let them go. The natives told them
that the Maker of heaven and earth
had his back up and was angry, and
they wanted them to stay and
â 'Sknokum Wah Wah," which means
big pray, to pacify the "Almighty. And
they had to stay.
' Dr. C. W. Chamberlain of Tacoma, a
dentist, who has been copper mining in
the Prince William Sound country, was
a passenger on the Dora. He says that
at Kayak, about 175 miles west of Yak
utat, the shocks were so severe that
the tops of several mountains rolled
and tumbled into the ocean at their
base. This is a sandstone range and
great clouds of dust were raised by the
detachment and the slide. The Dora in
running along her usual course be
tween Yakutat and Bituya, where
there has been heretofore fifty fathoms
of water, stirred up the mud with her
wheel. It was reported that her keel
found the bottom, but Captain Johan
sen denies this. He says that the stuff
the Dora stirred up was as thick as
cream gravy, while the water on the
surface had the usual clear appearance.
Captain Johansen says he feels certain
there had been'a violent disturbance of
some kind beneath the ocean there.

The Lakeside Jockey Club at Ruby,
Indiana, was refused admittance to the
Congress. The Western Turf Associa
tion, a new racing track at Tanfaren
Park, San Mateo County, Cal., was
elected to membership in the Congress.
Licenses for jockeys were increased from
$15 to $25.

ERNEST W. MICHAELIS
WEDS MISS CARRIE LEESE
â¢ Â«
Pretty Ceremony at the Home of th>e Bride's
Parents in Monterey.
MR. AND MRS. ERNEST W. MICHAELIS.
MONTEREY, Sept. 20.â A home -wedding took place in this city this
morning, which, though very quiet, was one of the most noted af
fairs of the kind that has ever occurred hereabouts. Miss Carrie
Leese, daughter of Mr. and Mrs. J. R. Leese of this city, and
grandniece of that famous old Californian, General M. G. Vallejo,
was married to Ernest W. Michaelis of San Francisco, in the presence of
only the families of the bride and bridegroom. The ceremony took place at
10 o'clock. The house was elaborately decorated and a wedding breakfast
followed the ceremony.
The bride, who is a petite brunette of the pure Spanish type, looked
very lovely in' her white bridal gown. Mi.Â«=s Charlotte Michaelis, sister of the
bridegroom, attended the bride and E. Richard Leese acted as best man.
Mr. and Mrs. Michaelis took the noon train for San Francisco, where they
will reside.
The bridegroom is a member of the printing firm of Michaelis Bros, of
San Francisco and a son of Judge E. Michaelis, formerly of Monterey. He
was born in Monterey and lived here until about two years ago.

Long Ago Declared a Nuisance at a

Trustees.
BAKTCRSFTEI/D. Sept. 20.-YeÂ»terday
the Supervisors in special session directed
ihe District Attorney to bring suit in the
name of the people of California to
abate the Panama slouch, that meanders
for over two miles through the heart of
the city. A short time ago the City Trus
tees declared the slough a menace to
health, and therefore a nuisance. The
suit Is to be brought under the act of 1893.
U promises to be difficult to ascertain
who to bring action against.
The slough was at one time the bed of
a fork of tV Kern River. Years ago the
river changed Up course, and ever since
the slough has carried enough water to
cause It to be full of slush and putrefying
matter. Sometimes it is used to run irri
gat on water through, which water is
claimed by Miller & Lux. It is known
th-U he Kern County Land Company
wants it filled up. and Henry Miller wÂ«-nts
It kent open. Complicated litigation is
liable to arise from the action to abate it.
WARDEN AULL RE-ELECTED.
A General Cut in Salaries at the Fol
som Prison.
SACRAMENTO, Sept. 20.-The State
Prison Directors at Folsom to-day re
elected Captain C. Aull "Warden of the
prison for the ensuing four years. They
also decided to call for plans and bids
for the 115.000 sewage disposal plant. The
successful bidder will bo required to give
a bond to guarantee the successful ope
ration of hin plan. Prison labor will be
supplied on the work.
Owing to the Legislature having cut
down the salary appropriation, it was
found necessary to scale down the entire
list. The application of Convict Thomas
Reed of Nevada County for a parole was
postponed for future action.
Seeking His Children.
SAN RAFAEL, Sept. 20.-F. W. Neid
lein, an employe of the Southern Pacific
Company at Sacramento, came here to
day in search of his three children, who,
he alleges, were spirited away by his
wife. His two boys were located at St.
Vincent's Orphanage, where a mix-up oc
curred, so the husband says, between
himself and his wife, who happened on
the scene as the boys were about to be
taken.
Neidlein says his 13-year-old daughter
is now In San Jose, and as soon as he
finds her he will take the three children
home. The husband is now suing the
wife for divorce and ask? to be appointed
legal guardian of the children.

ONE of the most important matters of national legislation which will be submitted to the consideration of Congress at its next session is a revision of the war tariff, which, through certain inequalities and injustices, has been made
a question of immediate necessity The war tariff, as it is now fixed, has worked a serious injustice upon most of the people of the country. Gross discrimination has been shown in favor of the express and telegraph compa
nies which have been permitted through inadvertence, to escape their just share of taxation. In order to remedy this evil and to establish a more equitable distribution of tax burdens, Congress intends at its next session to
amend the war tariff law and force the shirkers to do what they refuse to do willingly-pay their just taxes in proportion to the value of their holdings and the volume of their business.
The matter is of such -eneral importance that The Call has sent to each member of the California Congressional delegation a letter of inquiry designed to learn from each his opinions on the proposed amendment to the law.
The answers to these inquiries are of vital public interest. Without exception they show that the national legislators, who represent this State, believe that the express companies and the telegraph companies are evading their obliga
tions and are escaping taxation which they should endure. , , , . -iijtj^
When the war revenue law was adopted by Congress there was of necessity considerable haste in the proceedings. The Government needed money, and the bill providing for increased revenues was quickly drafted and
adopted It was natural therefore 'that there should' be certain ambiguities of expression, and some oversights in the bill. The national legislators who adopted the law are frank to admit these facts and to acknowledge with regret that
the express companies and the telegraph companies have taken advantage of the Government's necessity to place upon the shoulders of people, already heavily burdened, new obligations.
These corporations have refused persistently to pay the tax which the National Government decided they should pay. They have forced their patrons to pay the tax and where the injustice was resented the companies have de
clined to accept either messages or packages. In this they have made themselves liable to the forfeiture of their franchises as common carriers, but they have risked the liability and defied the National Government.
This defiance has meant to them a tremendous profit. They have been made defendants in courts from the lowest to the highest jurisdiction and invariably they have been beaten, defeated by decisions which declared that they
and not the business men who are their patrons should pay the tax. On this coast the chief offender and the one most impudent in its operations has been Wells, Fargo & Co. This corporation, in its contempt of the spirit and the mean
in- of the law has outdone perhaps even' other powerful offender in the country. It is natural, therefore, that the representatives of California in the National Legislature should consider the problem of equitable taxation a vital one.
In it, letter to the members of the California Congressional delegation The Call calls attention particularly to the necessity of a revision of the war revenue law and the purposeof the national legislature to make the revision. Refer
ence is made to the persistent violations of the law by Wells. Fargo & Co. and other corporations, and the opinions of each Congressman and United States Senator on the subject are requested.
The answers to these letters are given in what follows. In every instance the reply shows that the Congressional representatives of California are unanimous in their opinion that the telegraph and express companies, and not
their patrons should bear the tax. Every representative of California in Congress believes that the war revenue law should be revised to remedy the evil, and each pledges himself to vote for and agitate such a revision. All are emphatic
in declaring that the tax burden should be borne by the companies and not by business men or private individuals. Those who are familiar with the details of the legislation which resulted in the adoption of the war revenue law insist that
the law intended to tax such corporations as express and telegraph companies, and that the refusal to pay the tax is based solely upon a technicality.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER gl. 1899.
THE LETTER ADDRESSED BY THE CALL TO
CALIFORNIA'S CONGRESSIONAL DELEGATION
DEAR SIR: There seems to be a consensus of opinion that one of the first things to which
Congress wUI direct its energies after convening will be the revision of the war revenue /ate. In
the haste of the preparation of the act to meet the needs of the nation ambiguities of language were
committed which defeated the intentions of Congress and gave the express and telegraph compa
nies a pretext for refusing to pay their just share of the War tax.
] Tells, Fargo & Co. has persistently refused to pay the war tax upon the receipts issued to
its customers, except to newspapers and a few favored shippers, as required by the act of Congress.
The telegraph companies have taken advantage of the defects in the act to shirk the payment of their
part of the tax and thereby force their patrons to bear the burden.
In the 'revision of the War Revenue Law, will you favor an amendment which ivill be de
signed to compel the express companies to pay the war tax on receipts issued to shippers and the
telegraph companies to pax the war tax on all messages transmitted over their lines?
The Call will be glad to receive and publish your views upon the subject. Yours truly,

DECREE GRANTING
PARDON TO DREYFUS
PARIS, Sept. 21.â The Journal OfÃ
ciel to-day publishes the decree grant
ing pardon to Dreyfus. In a report
preceding the decree the Marquis de
Galfifet, Minister of War," points out
that Dreyfus has already undergone
five years' deportation, but that as the
law does not assimilate his deportation
with seven years' solitary confinement,
the prisoner would have to undergo ten
years' detention.
The Minister also calls attention to
the fact that the health of the prisoner
is seriously compromised and that he
would not be able, without great dan
ger, to undergo prolonged detention.
The report of the War Minister con
cludes thus:
"The Government will not have met
the wishes of the country, which de
sires pacification, if it does not hasten
to efface all traces of the painful con
flict. It belongs to you, M. le Presi
dente, by an act of lofty humanity, to
give the first pledge of the work of ap
peasement which opinion demands and
the good of the republic commands."

to another train and arrived at their des
tination two hours late.
The train was in charge of Conductor
Engwieht and Engineer Alger, to whom
no lilume is attached. It Is considered
fortunate that the train was slowing
down when the wheel broke. All the pas
sengers were more or less shaken up and
momentarily scared, but they accented
their unavoidable delay good naturedly.

JEW BAITER
CAUSES RIOTS
IN ALGIERS
Bloodshed Follows the At
tempt of Regis to Imitate
Guerin.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
ALGIERS, Sept. 21.â Max Regis,
former Mayor of Algiers, and a noto
rious Jew baiter, who returned here re-
I cently, haunted by the idea that the
Government intended to arrest him and
Jealous of the notoriety of Jules Guerin,
I shut himself up for four days with a
i party of adherents in a village, labeled
I "Anti-Juif," just outside the town. He
laid in a stock of provisions for a siege.
Yesterday, apparently annoyed at the
absence of any move on the part of the
police, he issued forth and entered the
town, stirring up anti-Jewish demon
strations, during which the windows of
several shops owned by Jews were
smashed. The Jews responded by fir
! ing revolvers and the mob rallied the
cry "Death to the Jews."
The riots continued throughout the
evening and finally the troops charged
with drawn swords and dispersed the
! rioters. Regis regain the villa and bar
| ricaded himself and his companions.
During the affray nine persons were
j stabbed or shot, among them three po-
I lice agents and two inoffensive citizens.
Took Strychnine by Mistake.
SAN DIEGO, Sept. 20.â Mrs. M. D. Sun
nocks of this city, wife of a prominent
contractor, died from strychnine poison
this evening. She was suffering from a
cold and wished to take a doae of qui
nine. By mistake she filled a capsule
with strychnine and swallowed it. Death
resulted in fifteen minutes. She was 53
years of age.

Next to the war itself and the incidents
directly connected with it. the wonderful
revolution that has occurred in business

which came to hand

OPPOSES THE
FILIPINO WAR
Senator Mason Attacks
Administration.
Special DiarÂ«tch to The Call.
WASHINGTON", Sept. 20.â 1n an Inter
view to-day Senator Mason of Illinois
said:
"On the floor of the Senate I shall con
tinue my opposition to the war upon the
Filipinos. l would sooner resign ray seat
than treat a dog the way we are treat
ing those people. , 1 am ashamed of my
country. People make a great mistake
when they say that 1 am against expan
sion. I am not. But expansion by pur
chase or other honorable means is one
thing; expansion by force is another. I
do not believe that there was any neces
sity for this war upon the Filipinos. I
believe that if we had adopted a resolu
tion in Congress assuring those people
as we assured the Cubans, that we had
no sellish designs upon them, we never
would have met with any opposition in
the Philippines. The fact is that we have
given the Filipinos every reason to dis
trust us and they are lighting for their
own liberty.
"Talk about hauling down our flag.
Will our flag come down in Cuba? Cer
tainly It will If we are true to our prom
ises. And the day that it comes down
will be the brightest day in our history,
because if will show that we are a great,
unselfish nation devoted to the eternal
principles of right and honesty. If the
Cubans want to be annexed later, that
is another question. Why cannot we deal
with the Filipinos in the same way?"

ADMITS HIS GUILT AND
FREES HIS ACCOMPLICES
Sensational Incident During the Trial
of the Man Who Shot the Late
King Milan.
BELGRADE, Sept. 20.â The trial of
GjuraKnezevic, the Bosnian who attempt
ed to assassinate former King Milan of
Servia last July, and of the influential
radical leaders, charged with being ac
complices in th*- attack, developed to-day
a sensational Incident.
Standing before the Bible, and crucifix,
Knesevlc formally withdrew all accusa
tions of complicity. He said;
"I charged these prisoners with com
plicity in order to save myself. I swear
now that these men are absolutely inno
cent. I lired on Milan out of personal re
venge because I thought he was the cause
of my falling to find employment. I have
now freed my soul. It is for you Judges
to decide."

QUIET PREVAILS IN CUBA.
General Wood Completes an Investi
gation of the Provinces.
SANTIAGO. Sept. 20.â General "Wood's
inspection of the provinces was concluded
Monday. He says that he found the coun
try quiet throughout and the people will
ing to work, but little progress has been
made since the war. The general was
given a warm reception at Puerto Prin
cipe.
The hospital at San Juan Dedios is in
.bad condition and General Wood will
grant $400 to complete it.

DR. KL^ME&'S BEMEDIES. . I
DR. KILMER'S SWAMP-ROOT
Is Not Recommended for Everything.
But it You Have Kidney or Bladder
Trouble It Will Be Found Just
the Remedy You Need.
Kidney trouble preys upon the mind,
discourages and lessens ambition;
beauty, vigor and cheerfulness soon
! disappear when the kidneys are out of
order or diseased.
Kidney trouble has become so preva-
lent that it is not uncommon for a
child to be born afflicted with weak kid-
neys. If the child urinates too often,

Strange Case of a Sister
and Brother.
V
Soecial Dispatch to The Call
PASADENA. Sept. 2").â To-day a brother
and sister who had never known each
other were united through the medium
of a newspaper advertisement.
Sixteen years ago Mr. and Mrs. Can
ton died In New York. They left three
childrenâ Albert J., Essie M. and William.
Albert was 12 and Essie 4 and William
j a babe In arms. The grandparents, who
were too old to properly care for the
orphans, took themin charge. Later Al
bert was adopted by a Mr. Wheeler and
! took his name. The girl was adopted by
i a family named Chase and known up bs-
Bie M Chase William stayed with his
'; grandparents and kept his right name.
Time wore on and the children separ
: ated. Albert went to Bridgeport and Es
j sic came to Pasadena, residing with her
adopted mother. Mrs. Sarah K. chase, on
' Park street. The brothers and sister
knÂ«w nothing of each other for a long
time Then Albert, being told about his
i little sister, of whom he had a vague rec
ollection, had a desire to see her. He
found she had loft New York and come
to the Pacific Coast. Twice he traveled

turned unsuccessful. Then he advertised
In a New York newspaper. A relative
I of Mrs. Chase, now living in Stratford.
â  Conn read the advertisement, which
! stated that Wheeler\s former name was
' canton, and gave all the facts he knew

i known here as Essie Chase. Albert has
! been corresponding with her and they

ito be brother and sister. Their meeting

MAYORS DISCUSS
MUNICIPAL WELFARE
Atlanta, Ga., Delegates Working to
Secure the Convention for
Their City Next Year.
SYRACUSE. N. T.. Sept. 20.âDele
gates to the convention of the League
of American Municipalities were late in
assembling to-day. Prior to the ses
sion representatives of Charleston, S.
C. ; Atlanta, Ga., and Peoria, 111., busied
themselves in soliciting support of the
claims of their respective cities for the
convention next year. Secretary Gilki
son announced that forty Mayors have
registered and 114 cities have represen
tation at the convention.
Mayor J. A. Johnson of Fargo, N. D.,
discussed "Special Assessment for City
Work," reviewing the laws authorizing
such assessments in various countries,
but more particularly those pertaining
to such legislation in the United States.
Of the forty-five States in the Union,
fnrty States and one Territory had laws
permitting the levying of special as
sessments to pay for local or special
improvements. Some difference existed
as to the method of procedure among
so many States, but the main principle
of special assessments was recognized
in them all.
Other interesting addresses were
made.

BIG SLUMP OCCURS
IN MILLING STOCK
Sensation in Wall Street Caused by
the Recent Death of Miller
Pillsbury.
NEW YORK, Sept. 20.â Wall street
was treated to a sensation to-day by
the United States Flour and Milling
; shares. Cm Tuesday the common stock
! closed at 66. To-day It sold at 24. The
i preferred, which closed at 75 on the pre-
' ] ceding day, dropped to ;"0. Their net
I declines for the day were 3 to 25 points,
| respectively. These breaks were made
\ on light transactions, only 1100 shares
I of the common and 1300 shares of the
preferred changing hands. The street
was much exercised over the declines,
j although very little of either stock is
held by commission houses or by spec
ulators at large.
The cause of the slump of the Flour
i and Milling was the liquidation of long
I stock partially by insiders and the
withdrawal of all inside support. Offi
cers of the company stated that certain
! insiders who have had buying orders
: In the stocks ever since they were listed
1 found that the general weakness of the
I stock market was inducing liquidation
; by trade interests, and that the death
of ('. A. Pillsbury might also lead to
' liquidation. They consequently with
drew their orders and left the stocks
to take care of themselves.
Thomas A. Mclntyre, treasurer of the
company, who is regarded in Wall
I street as the most important of the di
i rectors, said:
"There is no real reason for such a
I decline. There is nothing that war
i rants it and present prices by no means
| express the value of the stocks. We are
j making money. Present earnings are,
' however, surplus for the common stock.
! I do not think that insiders will long
neglect the opportunities presented by
j the slump."

New York Promoters of Pugilism

CASHIER BEATEN
WITH A HAMMER
Brutal Attack on an Old
Man in Palatine.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CHICAGO, Sept. 20.â Frederick J.
Filbert, the aged cashier of the Pala
tine Bank, in Palatine, 111., twenty-six
miles north of Chicago, is lying close
to death as the result of an attack
made upon him to-day by a young man
who gives the name of Walter Lawton.
The latter, who is unknown to any
body in Palatine, and apparently of
good education, is in the County Jail
suffering from a bullet wound in the
abdomen, which will probably prove
fatal.
Henry Plagge, 70 years of age, a
farmer, whose intervention at a critical
moment prevented the outright murder
of the cashier, is at his home, west of
the village, cut and bruised and dis
abled, as a result of his struggle with
Lawton, whose motive for the assault,
according to his repeated statements,
was not robbery.
The attack upon Filbert was made
with a tack hammer, and the cashier
was struck at least a dozen times be
fore Plagge interfered. The attack oc
curred at 3 o'clock in the afternoon.
Cashier Filbert was alone in the bank
when Lawton, whom he recognized as
a visitor of last Friday, who had
eought a loan on a supposed farm, en
tered. Lawton called Filbert into the
outer office to consult a map which
hung on the wall, and started a quar
rel. Filbert is small and feeble and
could offer little resistance to Lawton,
who pounded him savagely on the head
with a small tack hammer.
Repeated blows from the sharp ham
mer cent the old man to the floor, but
his cries attracted Farmer Plagge. As
the latter entered he was met by Law
ton, who said: "Filbert is in the back
room and I guess there is something
the matter with him."
Plagge pushed open the door, and as
he stooped over the prostrate cashier
Lawton struck him three times on the
back of the head with the hammer.
The old farmer turned on his knees and
grasped his assailant about the legs.
Both fell to the floor and struggled for
the possession of the hammer.
Each in turn received blows with it
until Lawton drew his revolver. The
men fought for the weapon, and in the
scuffle for it It was discharged, the ball
striking Lawton in the abdomen.
Plaggo said he had no idea of how the
revolver was discharged.
The wound weakened Lawton so
much that, although ho still fought.
Plagge had little trouble in throwing
him to the floor and holding him until
assistance arrived.
The crowd which gathered rapidly
after the pistol shot separated the two
men and took the supposed robber to
the village look-up. He was too severe
ly wounded to offer any resistance, and
this fact was all that saved him from
violence at the hands of the excited
crowd that followed him to jail.

SUISSER STILL
IN SAN JOSE
Officers Refuse to Sur
render Him.
*
THEY FEAR A LYNCHING
â - â â¦
TAKES A CHEERFUL VIEW OF

Is Confident of Securing an Acquittal

Sueclal Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, Sept. 2n.â George Suisser,
who killed Sheriff Farley of Monterey
County in cold blood, still maintains his
braggadocio manner. He laughs and chats
about the killing, ajid his (.-rime appears
to be a light matter to him. To-day he
said he killed Farley by mistake, but
reiterated that it was his intention to
kill Delaney and Allen.
This forenoon Constable Allen of Sa
linas visited the jail and demanded
Sui?ser, whom he stated he wanted to
take back to Salinas, He presented a
warrant for Suisser, charging him with
the murder of rarley, which had been
sworn to by ex-District Attorney P. E.
Zabaja. I'nder Sheriff Varcoe and Jailor
Rives refused to turn over the prisonei
t<> the Constable. Coroner Muller, who
jis acting Sheriff since Farley's death,
was communicated with by telephone,
i and he told the officers to hold Suisser.
He has another warrant for the murderer,
; and besides he did not deem it wise to
1 bring him back to Salinas. Allen was
greatly disappointed.
Constable Allen went in to see Suisser,
and the latter expressed his opinion of
j the officer in not very elegant language.
, He told Allen that he had been hounding
him for tin- purpose of milking a few dol
lars in fees. "1 would have killed you,
! all right, if it had not been dark and
foggy. When I shot all 1 could see was
I the flash ot your pistol. You are not big
enough to take me bark to Salinas, for
] would beat the face off you. You could
never take me alive."
Suissf-r says he is unable to account for
; the killing of Farley. He says he did
, not know that that officer was looking
for him. Allen and Nesbitt he knew were
j after him, and he supposed he was shoot
ing at those officers. He claims he phot
i to protect himself, as the officers had no
right to go after him, as he had done
nothing.
Suisser says he will not plead crullty
and will fight for his life in the courts.
He has not engaged attorneys yet, but
expects to secure able counsel. His plea
will be temporary insanity, caused by
drink and persecution. When drinking
he says he becomes insane, and all the
people in Salinas know it. He intimates
the officers should have steered clear of
I him while he was in this condition. He
| will also attempt to prove Farley's death
i was accidental, and that he merely re
turned shots that were fired at him in
defense.
Constable Allen returned to Salinas this
afternoon.
LESS FROM KLONDIKE.
Gold Output Will Be Much Decreased
This Year.
NEW YORK, Sept. 20.â The Canadian
Banl: has just received word from a trust
worthy mine owner that the yield from
the Klondike mines this year will be a
third less than last year's output. On
that basis the bank figures a probable
Yield this season of about $S.Cmxi.oÂ»)O.
Negotiations are already under way trans
ferring to this city payments for a con
siderable portion of the Klondike gold re
ceived at Seattle and San Francisco. Two
million dollars on this account will prob
ably be added to the reserves of local
banks ten days hence.
HOSPITAL BURNED.
Two Patients Believed to Have Lost
Their Lives.
NORFOLK. Va., Sept. 21.â St. Vincent's
Hospital, built at a cost of over $250,000,
was destroyed by fire at 2 o'clock this
morning.
Many of the patients were removed with
difficulty, and two who are missing are
supposed to have perished. One is an
Insane man and the other a woman. The
fire originated in the south wing on the
fifth floor.

IMPORTANT STREET DECISION
A Contractor's Ignorance of the Law-
Causes His Considerable Loss.
William Ede, a street contractor,
brought an action in the Superior Court
against Joseph Cuneo and other property
owners for the foreclosure of a lien
against their property on a street assess
ment. In tl, - lower court Ede received
judgment. Final judgment was defeated
for the reason that the City Engineer
had never made an engineers certificate
of the work. It seems that the Super
intendent of Streets had tiled the assess
ment, diagram, warrant, and what pur
ported to be the engineer's certificate,
which had never been made. Notwith
standing that this was not duly, prop
erly or legally recorded, the contractor
went on with" the assessment. The Su
preme Court takes the position that pro
ceedings in street assessment are purely
statutory.
When the contractor found that there
was an irregularity in the record he
should have demanded his right, that the
engineer's certificate should be a part of
the record. He went on and accepted
such documents as the Street Superin
tendent delivered to him. In falling to
so do he lost his case. The opinion, which
is of no small importance, was written
by Justice Harrison and concurred in by
Justices Henshaw. Temple and McFar
land. Chief Justice Bcatty and Justice
Van Dyke dissented on the broad ground
that because the Street Superintendent
failed to perform his duty the contractor
should not be made to suffer the loss of
his labor and material.
- â  â¦ â¢
Rough on the Contractor
Joseph Wtstall, a street contractor, is
paying dearly for his ignorance of the
law. After performing the work ot a
certain street Improvement he sued Lud
wig Altschul and others for the assess
ment. In the lower court he won, but
the judgment was reversed by the higher
court because of certain informalities
and irregularities in the procedure. On
the remittltur going down he caused the
action to be dismissed and began an ac
tion for a reassessment. Again he won
in the lower court and again he lost in
the Supreme Court, which yesterday re
versed the decision on the ground that a
reassessment can only be made ninety
days after a linal judgment and a re
versal of a case in the Supreme Court
Is not a final judgment. Under these cir
cumstances he is shut out from recover
ing for his work by the statute of limi
tations.

Ten Minute Service to Continue
Superintendent Jordan of the butro elec
tric railroad line from Central avenue to
the Cliff House has issued orders to the
division superintendents to continue the
schedule of service during the winter
months which means that ten-minute
service will be continued during the en
tire winter, as was the case during the
summer months. Heretofore it was the
custom to take off two cars during the
winter and run fifteen-minute cars, but
this year Superintendent Jordan will con
tinue the summer schedule throughout
the entire year.

OUR IRRIGATION PROBLEMS.
W; HILE the petition to the Secretary of Agricul
ture requesting him to have El wood Mead be
ns of the department into
is ot the country, by an examina
e of California, has not been wholly com
ith, enough has been granted to be gratifying
petitioners. -Mr. Mead's sen-ices will be re
ington for the general supervision -jf
the work, but Professor H. H. Hirst of the University
i has been deputed to direct the investiga
tions here, and provision has been made for the em
ployment of assistants as far as may be necessary to
procure the prompt completion of the work.
It would have been pleasing, of course, to have had
Mr. Mead make the California investigations in per
son, since as the official chosen to make the inquiry
he could have acted with more freedom than a sub
te and probably would have carried out the
work on a more comprehensive scale, but in other
respects the change is hardly to our disadvantage.
Professor Hirst has a larger acquaintance with the
State than Mr. Mead, is already familiar with many
in the irrigation problems his chief would have
â¢ iearn, and has naturally a keener interest in
:;ia. These advantages largely counteract any
disappointment that results from not having the chief
of the investigation to conduct the work in
and the petition, therefore, may be regarded
iving been virtually granted.
The benefits to be derived from the inquiry are
many. The Government furnishes the money re
quired to employ competent men to make a compre
hensive survey of the State and a careful study of all
the conditions that affect irrigation enterprises. When
the information is obtained, collated and digested, it
will be much easier for us to devise means for over
coming all the difficulties, whether natural or legal,
that now prevent the extension of irrigation on a
large scale in many sections of the State where it is
needed.
It is safe to say the two issues of greatest moment
to Californians are the protection of forests and the
conservation and economic use of watercourses. The
two things are but parts of one and the same problem,
and on the proper solution of that problem depends
not only the development of our prosperity, but its
maintenance. All intelligent men understand the im
portance of entering at once seriously upon the task
of dealing with those issues in a scientific manner,
and it is a matter of no little satisfaction that the
National Government has given its aid in obtaining
the data from which conclusions can be drawn as <o
the best methods of overcoming the difficulties in the
way.
As was expected, the story of the private soldier
sent to prison by General Otis for writing a letter to
the general has been explained by later information,
and it appears the reason for his imprisonment is not
that he wrote the letter but that he forged the names
that were signed to it. The papers that have made
the story an occasion for attacking Otis owe him an
apology, but they won't give it.
The war that Shakespeare knew may be the same
article that is threatening in the Transvaal, but there
it room to doubt it. The Bard of Avon wrote of the
kind that had a wrinkled front. The Boers may be
preparing to serve out that sort, but they have put

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL. THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 21, 1899.
CONSUMPTION QUARANTINE.
SINCE the proposition to qnarantine consump
tives was made by our State Board of Health
much desirable discussion has been stimulated
all over the country.
The disease is costly to civilization and appalling
in its results, because it strikes at human life in its
i most useful period. The diseases of senility, which
find in the weakness of old age an invitation to at-
I tack, strike at victims whose years are near accom
plished. Society has benefited from their physical
strength and mental vigor, and, these being ex
hausted, being already invested for the good of the
world, their call hence is a scene around which grati
tude for the past mourns sincerely, but society is not a
loser. "The young may die; the old must," is the
statement of a physical fact, and not a cynicism.
Medical science does its duty to the world by striv
ing to curb juvenile mortality. The odium of urban
life is the frightful mortality of children, not the nat
ural passing of the aged. The strength of states is
undermined when the young die in masses. What is
sorrow to a family in which children perish untimely
finally becomes to the commonwealth a menace.
When vital statistics are carefully kept and the birth
rate and juvenile death rate can be compared, the
future of a state can be accurately predicted and the
hour of its fall foretold. It is in this aspect that con
sumption is invested with a peculiar terror, and herein
is the interest of the state in its extirpation. It is a
scourge of the young. The period of its assault lies
in the twenty years between the ages of fifteen and
thirty-five.
This is the acre of adolescence and accomplishment.
The dynamic force of a nation is in its people between
those asres. Not only is that the period of their best
preparation for usefulness, but it is the period in
which the fullness of their strength passes into the
generations to be. Within that period are the pa
rental years, and the endurance needed in the nurture
of the young. Therefore, when consumption smites
a life it strikes society and the state. If it remove a
life before it reaches its majority parents may be left
forlorn for need of children to lean upon and depend
upon. Indigence follows, therefore, in the ghastly
train of consequences. If the disease cast its awful
shadow upon parents it falls across the cradle, and
infant life inherits constitutional weakness and is or
phaned at one blow.
An investigation of the final cause of crime, in
a high percentage of cases, finds behind the criminal,
poverty, and next behind its squalor, death, and next
in rank behind the grim specter gnaw the diseases
that slay between the ages of fifteen and thirty-live.
No social prudence, nor statutory care, nor institu
â  rt. can effectively replace and substitute the
;n the chain of cause and effect, which is broken
by the preventable diseases that destroy in that period
of twenty years. It is, therefore, the span which
science must patrol as a sentry, set to guard the life
of the state against an enemy.
We teach law and medicine in our State universi
ties â why? That the State may train men and women
to defend it in the war against crime and the war
against death. It is not merely to equip them, at
public expense, to make personal profit in a learned
profession, but to raise up a loyal guild whose mem
bers shall save to the State the strength of its people
in their most serviceable years, and shall preserve its
noblest crop, its oncoming men and women. Every
life saved in that period of twenty years economizes
for the State by lessening the risk of indigence and
shortening the calendar of the criminal court. In
place of paupers to be fed and criminals to be chained
it puts men who walk uprightly, filled with the sap of
life, gendered by a stout ancestry, and to be the fore
bears of a strong posterity. In these interests the
State takes its jurisdiction and derives among its po
lice powers the right to curb an endemic infection.
We have no doubt, as a proposition of law, that the
State can enforce isolation, the disinfection of sputum
and other useful sanitary rules, to limit the infection
of consumption. Typhoid fever is a secondary
scourge, but the police statutes of the State canonize
against the pollution of potable waters by dejections
and other means of charging them with the microbe of
typhoid. The State makes such pollution a misde
meanor and lays upon it a heavy hand. But the
State, as yet unawakened to the fact that pollution of
the air is easier than pollution of water, has no statute
against excretion of sputum in the street filled with
vital germs of consumption that are sure to reach the
air passages of unsuspecting hundreds, to find their
first lodgment and germinate in the larynx, produc
ing that peculiar sore throat that foreruns consump
tion, from there to spread and destroy the lung. The
State provides inspectors to watch the moths that
deposit germs in the bloom of fruit trees, whence is
sue worms that penetrate and destroy the pulp. The
origin and progress of the bacilli of consumption are
not widely different.
Of course a State quarantine raises a series of inter
esting legal questions. It is a restraint of personal
freedom. If established its first case would take out
a writ of habeas corpus, and the issue would go to
judicial trial. A decision would probably overthrow
the quarantine, but it would greatly illuminate the
problem and be of inestimable value in focusing
public attention upon its importance.
Hon. John Barrett, former Minister to Siam, is au
thority for the statement that Secretary Hay warned
him, just prior to his departure from Washington, to
be careful of his utterances, the inference left to be
deduced being that Barrett might unthinkingly let
out a state secret or two. Let Secretary Hay be as
sured to the contrary. A? long as Mr. Barrett pep
pers the intellectual feast he offers with the liberal
doses of the personal pronoun he has used so far on
his tour the country is safe.
THE WASTE OF THE FORESTS.
SOMETHING of gratification will be felt by all
who appreciate the value of our forests in the
announcement that the Government has under
taken a thorough investigation of the forests on the
national reserves with the intention of prosecuting
all persons guilty of wasting them or illegally cutting
timber from them.
One of the officers of the Government employed in
the investigation who has been at work in Washing
ton reports the discovery of extensive frauds car
ried out by timber companies under the forms of law
and with such cunning as will seemingly prevent
their prosecution. The inspector in describing the
operations of the frauds is reported to have said:
"Prior to the establishment of reserves many thou
sands of acres of valuable timber lands were patented
by logging and lumber companies. To such as held
deeds the Government offered to give an equal acre
age of timber lands at other points, but neglected to
set a date of exchange. These great companies at
once put to work an extra force of men and by keep
ing them hard at work soon had their patent cleared
of all timber; then under the law they presented the
land to the Government, selecting in return the most
valuable timber land that could be secured. In other
words, they have been 'double shooting the turn.' and
in many cases where it was the intention of the Gov
ernment to preserve a timbered area it has on its
' hands nothing but a stump acreage wholly devoid of
material for parks."
Of course where cutting has been done in that way
it will not be easy to prove criminal intent on the
part of the crafty companies. The Government was
at fault in giving them the option to exchange their
lands for others at any time that suited their con
venience. Naturally they have taken advantage of
the option to make all the profit possible without re
; gard to the waste of the forests or the fairness of the
exchange.
While it is not likely anything can be done to
prosecute past offenders in that way, it will be in the
power of the Government to prevent similar frauds in
the future, and that appears to be the intent of the in
vestigation. The work of prevention, moreover, can
not be undertaken too early, for, as the inspector
says: "The forest reserves of Western Washington
are badly broken up, and it will take years of culti
vation and care before they are as good as those in
the eastern part of the State, which were not so heav
ily patented when the law was passed."
The activity of the Government in this respect is
the more gratifying because it is in line with the gen
eral movement for forest protection throughout the
Union. Of course the National Government cannot
act everywhere, but it can set an example to the
States which those that are under the control of en
lightened legislators will follow.
By way of preventing white men from marrying all
the heiresses in the tribe the Chickasaw Legislature
has fixed the license for such marriages at $iooo.
; and the example might be followed with profit in New
York to check the drain of rich girls from that city
by titled Britons.
GOVERNMENT IN ASIA.
THE country needs that men who influence pub
lic opinion stand by their guns. President
Schurman of Cornell says now: "I consider
it a pity that the Filipinos do not recognize that the
best thing in the world for them would be to acknowl
edge American sovereignty."
When sovereignty has to be forced upon a people
, by conquest and arms, no matter if it appear to the
sovereign the best tiling in the world for the subjects,
they never take it that way. Therefore it has to be
: maintained over them by force. The sovereignty, oi
' necessity, becomes a military despotism, and the
. slightest relaxation of military vigilance is at once
met by an outbreak and armed resentment We are
going to put down existing resistance in the Philip
pines. There is no doubt of that, but when it is done
we are over only the least of our troubles in that
â  quarter. American contempt for what we regard as
i the inferior races was never more active than now.
It seems to be an established trait in our national
character. It is that sentiment which made our men
in the Philippines feel that the natives had none of
the rights of human beings on the soil where they
', were born. Holding others and inferiors in subjec
tion has never yet deepened the noble characteristics
of any people. The master has always degenerated
and the subject has always suffered.
In August, 1898, President Schurman said: "This
republic, whose soul is self-government, does not
want Asiatic dependencies or the military despotism
they would entail. The proximity of Cuba made its
misgovemment our affair, but we are not called upon
to rectify the tyrannies of India or of Asia. Nor are
we under any obligations to Aguinaldo and the- in
surgents. Let us keep to our own hemisphere, seek
ing only naval stations in the Oid World."
As Commissioner Schurman he now counsels a
I course that leads straight to a military despotism as
i the only form of government possible of institution in
those distant islands. The effect of English rule in
Asia was for years the subject of unfavorable com
ment in this country. Now that has ceased, and our
imperialists point to that rule as a model of benefi
cence and an example that lures us to imitation.
One Sunday last May something was said in West- |
bourne Park Chapel, London, to a "British audience
about empire in India. The speaker was Dadabhai ;
Navoji, a Hindoo, who represents an English con- j
i stituency in the House of Commons. He said that as I
the United States, hitherto a devotee of the Monroe !
doctrine, had abandoned it and appeared to share in I
the imperialism of the world, the American people (
would look to imperial government in India as an I
example to follow or to avoid. Then followed a ter
rible arraignment of England's government in India, j
He said: "An oriental despot when he misgoverned
acted, so to speak, like a butcher, and people were
astounded and horrified; but this new despotism of j
civilization rather resembled a murder effected by a
clever but unscrupulous surgeon who drew all the
blood from his victim while leaving scarcely a soir j
upon his skin. Moreover, if under oriental despotism j
the results to the individual were serious, they at
least were not so terrible to the country. A par
ticular victim was no doubt despoiled of his fortune,
but some favorite benefited and the money at least
', remained in the country, whereas the British system
of despotism took away year by year a greater por
tion of the wealth of India, with the result that to
day the Indians are the poorest people in the world.
Lord George Hamilton talked glibly of the manner in
which British empire in India had been built up by
the spilling of British blood and the spending of Brit
ish treasure. But much of the blood . was Indian,
while, as to the treasure, not a farthing of it was Brit
j ish. They had, on the contrary, constrained ,the
wretched natives to contribute the whole cost, and
were still drawing from India year by year millions
; upon millions, to the still greater impoverishment
and destruction of the Indian people. Lord Salisbury
himself had said that India must be 'bled,' and ad
mitted that India was injured by the drain that was
constantly going on in the exportation of so much
revenue without any equivalent, and went on to say
I that the great mass of the people, the agricultural
! community, had no more blood remaining in them,
1 and the lancet must be applied where the blood was
I congested. The lot of the unhappy Indian is some
â  what worse than that of the negro slaves of America
in the old days, for the masters had an interest in
keeping them alive for their money value. But if an
Indian or a million of Indians died there was another
or other millions to take their places and be the
slaves of British officials in their turn. The average
j annual income per head of the people of the British
isles is Â£41; of the people of India it is 25 shillings
(six dollars and a quarter.)"
In other words, England is in India for commercial
purposes, and this country is asked to make Asiatic
i conquests for the same purposes. If these are gained.
j by what different process will they be accomplished?
The British and the Boers are doing as much fight
ing talk without fighting as any two prize ring cham
pions, and perhaps the challenging and counter chal
lenging will end in much the same way.

himself the journey and continued in the city splitting
hairs for the railroad company.

VOTE OF THE FARMERS ON EXPANSION
Verdict of 20,000 Farmers is Against Hold
ing the Filipinos in Subjection.
AS 70 per cent of the population of the United States still live In the country
and the small towns, the opinions of the dwellers in large cities on na
tional questions is of less relative weight than many might suppose. A
plebiscite of 20,000 farmers in all parts of the country on our expansion
problems, which has just been completed by Farm and Home (Springfield.
Mass.). has therefore unusual value. More than half of the farmers who
voted are from the Central West and the Middle States, which decided the 1895
election, and the rest are divided about equally between the New England, the
Southern and the Pacific Coast States, so that the whole country is represented.
Following are the most important of the questions and replies:
Should the Filipinos be held in subjection to the United States, or
should they be allowed to form an independent government?

THE LAND OF NODâ A. M. H., Cltv.
After Cain slew Abel he went to the Land
of Nod and took unto himself a wife, but

DIAMONDSâ H. and Q., City. Dia
monds are pure carbon crystallized. They
are the hardest of all known substances,
will scratch other minerals! and gems ana
can be cut only by their own dust. Dia
mond is infusible, but will burn at a high
temperature, as. for instance, before th'
blowpipe in air or in oxygen gas. When
burned it will not resolve into charcoal.
ASSUMED NAMEâ G. T. C. Oakland.
This correspondent asks: "If a person
was married under an assumed name
twenty yean ago, Is that marriage legal
now?" The question is too general to ad
mit of an answer, aH there may be con
ditions that would render the marriage as
valid as if the party bad given the true
name or there may be such that would
render It Invalid. Without a knowledge
of the facts of the case an answer cannot
be given.

to offer any position's in its offices, a3
there are more applicants than vacancies.
An applicant for any position in the com
pany should, unless he is acquainted wtih
some of the heads of departments, address
a communication to "The Southern Pa
cific Railroad Company, Market and
Montgomery streets, San Francisco, Cal.,"
setting forth desire of the applicant, qual
ifications and recommendations. It will
be referred to the proper department, and
a reply will be sent. The same course
should be followed as to the express com
pany asked about.
ETIQUETTE AND STATIONERYâ
Subscriber, Sulphur Creek, Colusa County,
Cal. Etiquette demands a certain adher
ence to conventional forms In regard to
the size and shape of the paper and en
velopes used in writing letters of friend
ship, congratulations, condolence, etc.
Beyond a compliance with this general re
quirement each individual can use a per
sonal preference as to what tint shall be
chosen, what style of monogram. if any,
shall be Impressed, and whether the
sheets of paper shall be plain or grained
and rough. A gentleman may have a
monogram on his paper and envelopes if
he so desires without transgressing on
! the rules of etiquette; that is a matter of
| individual taste, but the requisite Is that
the stationery be of good quality, the
handwriting plain and the style simple.
IMPEACHMENTâ J. H.. City. The Con
stitution of the United States, article I,
section 3, directs how the President and
other civil officers shall be tried. It pro
vides that "the Senate shall have the
sole power to try all impeachments. "When
sitting for that purpose the members shall
be on oath or affirmation. When the
I President of the United States is tried,
I the Chief Justice shall preside; and no
! person shall be convicted without the
concurrence of two-thirds of the members
present." Under this organic law, the
House of Representatives impeaches or
! makes the charges against the officer ac-
I cused, the articles being practically the
same as the counts in an indictment. The
impeachment is sent to the Senate, a
! copy is served on the accused and the
House of Representatives appoints a com
mittee of members as managers or prose
cutors of the impeachment. When the
Senate is convened as a court, the man
agers on the part of the House, and the
counsel for the accused, are summoned
to appear before it; and the trial pro
ceeds much the same as trials of other
causes of large interest. In the Impeach
; ment of Federal officers other than the
i President the same course Is followed cx
i cept that the President of the Senate pre
! sides.

MEN PRESENT AN ULTIMATUM
Â«.
WANT AN EIGHT HOUR DAY ON
GOVERNMENT WORK.
Ail Chartered Steamers Must Ec Con
sidered Government Vessels and
Pay Accordingly â The Work
Will Be Done Elsewhere.
â¢ â  . â  - .-- - â â¢ ,
The boiler makers' strike reached the
acute stago yesterday. The men want an
eight-hour day on all vessels chartered to
the Government, and if their demand la
not granted within twenty-four hours
they assert that the rule will be made
to apply to private contracts also. Uncle
Sam, they say, made the eight-hour law
ar.d he must live up to it. A private
contract on a Government ship must be
considered a Government job as far as
the hours of labor are concerned, and if
that point Is not conceded, then the eight
hour law will be made to apply to all
work done by boiler makers.
The above ultimatum was given yes
terday afternoon In the office of the
transport quartermaster on Folaom street
wharf. A conference had been called to
Bee whether some understanding could
not be reached. There were present Cap
tains Barneson and Buuhelder and Su
pervising- Engineer Matthews of the
transport service, H. C. Tabrett, marine
superintendent, and M. Arnold, manager
of the Risdon Iron Works; L. lieyne
mann, secretary; W. L. Criglar, marine
superintendent, and Peter P. Phelan,
head boiler maker of the Fulton Iron
Works; James Ryan, G. Lillis. Jack Mit
chell, Frtd Murphy and J. Furlong of
the Boiler Makers" Union, and John Bul
ger, United States Inspector of boilers.
Mr. Bulger was chosen as the arbitrator,
but in spite of his best efforts he could
not bring the two parties to an under
standing.
Captain Barneson in a straightforward,
luamy manner explained to tne men the
Government's position in the matter.
"Boys," said he, "1 want you to get your
rights as much as you do yourselves. As
far as the Government is concerned, I
think i can promise you, in the name of
Major Long, an eight-hour day and dou
ble pay for overtime. That of course
â¢wIU oniy refer to Government work. On
a chartered learner we have nothing
whatever to do with repairs to the boil
ers, except in the ordering of the work
done. For instance, we charter a vessel
and the lirst thing we do is to send an
inspector over her. He reports that the
borers require retublng, and we notify
the owner that the work must be done
before we will accept the vessel. Now
the owner of that vessel employs whom
be pleases to do the work and he foots
the bill. We have nothing whatever to
do with it, and 1 certainly fall to see
how you can make it out to be a Gov
ernment job.
â¢â¢On the Warren, Arizona, Grant, Sheri
dan. Sherman, Relief and other vessels
owned by the Government you will get
what you ask, but to my mind chartered
vessels like the George W. Elder, Ta
coma, Zc-alandla, Valencia, Centennial,
Char.- Nelson and others should be con
bidered ns outside Jobs, as the owners
have to pay for the repairs."
The boiler-makers listened to Captain
Barnepon quietly and then retired to talk
the matter over among themselves. In
tpector of Boilers Bulger went with them
and the whole matter was gone carefully
over. Mr. Bulger counseled the men to
ir;uke certain concessions, but he lulled
to influence the boiler-makers. They re
turned to the meeting room and gave it
us their ultimatum that any vessel com
ing here to enter the transport service
should be considered a Government vessel,
and the job a Government job.
For this they would demand ten hours'
pay for an eight-hour day and double pay
on that scale for overtime.
"Take care, boys, that you are not cut
ting oft your nose to spite your face." said
Captain Barneson. "I consider that you
are going the right way to drive trade
away from the city. Remember that we
cannot get American tonnage to transport
the troops, and that foreign vessels are
being- chartered in numbers. Remember
that there is nothing to prevent these for- I
eign owners from having their repair |
work done where they please. All we re- j
quire is that the transport will pass our
Inspectors. We do not care who does the
work so long as It is done in a workman- :
like manner. Remember that many of
these transports which the Government is
negotiating fcr are coming from Austra
lia and China, and that If you insist upon ;
your demands the owners will have the
work done at Sydney, N. S. W., or at
Hongkong, and for God's sake don't let it
go abroad that an American workingman !
held up his own Government in an emer- |
gency. '
There was a long pause after Captain
Barneson had finished and then Ryan,
acting as spokesman for the boller-mak
ere, said they would give the Iron works
people twenty-four hours to consider their I
proposals, and if their demands were not
granted In that time they would ask for
still further concessions.
The representatives of the different iron '
works are going- to sleep on the men's de
mands to-night and will give their dcci- I
sion to-morrow. In the meantime the !
steamer Bristol was to have come here i
for an overhauling, but she will now go '
to Seattle, where the work will be done. !
The Glenogle requires considerable work,
but she may he parsed by the inspectors
and the work will be done at Hongkong â 
or she may go to Puget Sound and be
outfitted there. The Lenox that was to i
come here from Portland may remain on
the Columbia River nnd be overhauled
there. All In all. the outlook is not very \
promising: for the port.
