JOE COCHRANE, HENCHMAN
OF CHRIS BUCKLEY, DEAD
OLD Joe Cochrane, who In the days of political chaos swung the Demo
cratic vote of the Barbary Coast in keeping with the dictates of the
"Blind White Devil," passed away at the City and County Hospital
yesterday morning. His death was not unexpected. It was simply the
fading away of a life spent in activity and good-fellowship, capped now and.
then and at last crowned with a period of dissipation, A few weeks ago it
was found that this dead henchman of Buckley was ill and penniless. It was
only after vigorous protestations that he consented to be' removed to- the
hospital, he thought â others knewâ to die. He did not survive long- after
entering 1 the public care, and yesterday the end came. Whether or not he
will -'Ind a pauper's grave remains to he seen. He has done much for some,
and !t Is true that he has "done" others; but still there are those who say
that some of the friends he befriended in days gone by should see that his
tomb is not marked with a number, as though he were numbered among the
unknown or friendless dead.
Joe Cochrane. first came to public notice through his efforts on behalf of
Chris Buckley when Buckley's position In the arena of politics was not to be
envied. When Buckley came into power, however. it seems that he forgot
Joe, but Joe never forgot his ingratitude. Deserted by Buckley, but stand
injr'on the name ho seined as a supporter of the "Blind White Dcvi!." he
passed many are hours selling positions on the police force to the passing
"Rubes." For each position/or rather offer, he exacted $400 from the would
be* "cop." No position would be forthcoming and then there would be trouble.
He was forced to defend himself against several of these actions, but always
escaped conviction. ! From the court he would go forth to the street and sell
another position on the force, but finally things became too warm in that
line and Cochrane abandoned the pursuit Shortly after he began the not
unusual occupation of giving the uninitiated experience in the line of police
duty for the sum of **00 each. Buckley for a. time again picked him up. Then
Cochrane spent his wealth grained from many fruitful sources "perpetuating
Chris" At "the same time. Chris was perpetuating himself, banking his
money and buying bonds. Chris is still alive and wealthy, but the last food
Cochrane lifted to his lips was purchased from the coffers of public charity.
San Francisco Labor Council respectfully
asks you to publish the following: Th->
strike of the boilermakers on the trans
ports for ihe eight-hour day will, if not
settled shortly, result in the delay of
these transports. Tills the- hoilermukers
regret, but as they" only ;; <lc for the rights
wanted thÂ«m by the Federal eight-hour
law. rights now granted by the contract
ors to the members of the other trades
I needed in the repair of the transports,
i the beiiermakers cannot be held re
sponsible for this delay. They believe
that the Government in letting thes.- con
tÃ has allowed the contractors
emergency prices, and they refuse to be
discriminated against any longer.
Granted the eight-hour Jay, they nr.> will
ing, as they have done In the past, to
overtime.
On Septembor 9 the boilermakers em
ployed by the Risdon. the Fulton and th<-
Eureka boiler works made a successful
demand for the eight-hour day for work
on transports. The Union iron Works
alone continued to work its men ten
hours. On September 13 the secretrirv of
the i-abor Council, upon the urgent ap
peal of the Boilermakers' I'nion to the
j Labor Council, had Prank A. Hearn
swear to a complaint against the Union
Iron Works for the violation of th~ F.-d
--oral eight-hour law on '.he transport Han
cock, un September IT appeared a state
ment in the newspapers that on the
â  previous day the manager of the Union
ir>>n 'Works ha<l produced before United
States Commissioner Heacock letters
' from Washington to the i fleet that work
on the Hancock was emergency w..rk.
Thereupon the Commissioner dismissed
i the case. At 4 p. m. on the Bame day. the
j 16th, the boilermakers in the- Risdon
â  were asked to go back to the nine-hour
' system. This they refused and the
present strike is the result. The boiler
makers In the Fulton were also asked to
return to nine hours and likewise refused.
The Eureka boiler works, now repair
ing: tho transport Centennial, adhered to
the eight-hour system and its boilei-
I makers are now at work and the Centen
nial will suffer no delay.
Some statements have been made that
POLICE JUDGE THOMAS F. GRAHAM
the strike ot the bollermaUers wni cause
the transport work to be taken away
from San Francisco. There certainly is
no fear of that. The bo'lermakers on
Puget Sound, as well organized as those
here, will Insist on their rights, and be
-. the contractors in this city when
â¢ i nfronted with the loss of this work,
either to Pug^t Sound or Hongkong, will
quickly prant the boilermakers their just
demands.
Professor Ely once said: "The trouble
lies what to do with the twentieth man.
Nineteen employers will grant fair con
ditions to their workmen, the twentieth,
only considering his profits, refuses and
ultimately forces the other nineteen to
his level." Had the Union Iron Works
acted like Its competitors, this strike
would not have occurred.
I am, by order of the Executive Com
mittee of the San Francisco Labor
Council, Yours respectfully.
ED. ROSEN'UKRG.

dents Win a Victory.
â Â« â
VERDICT AGAINST LANDLORDS
TWINKLING STAR DIRECTORS
ARE CONVICTED.
â â¦ â
Jury Takes but Fifteen Minutes to
Reach a Decision â Sentence
Deferred One Week â Chief
Lees May Close Cribs.
The notorious Hotel Nymphla, corner of
Pacific and Stockton streets, has received
what should prove its death blow and the
residents of North Beach who have
worked day and night to obliterate this
festering sore from their midst have won
a well earned victory.
After a tria! extending over a month,
two weeks being occupied in obtaining a
jury, a verdict of guilty was obtained in
Judge Graham's court yesterday against
B. P. Blumenthal, L. P. E. Prey. Emile
i Kehrlein and Valentine H. Kehrlein, who
i with W. 11. Richards constitute the
board Of directors of the Twinkling Star
Improvement Company, the sweet-scented
corporation that ie responsible for the ex
istence of the infamous resort. The
charge on which these men were con
victed was "letting premises, knowing j
that the same were to be used for the pur
poses of prostitution," and the Jury that
rendered the verdict was composed of:
A. L. Alderson, L. Harter. F. V. Peterson,
M. Meyer, Henry Beckman, Edward Bul
lard, H. O. Tibblts, M. Brandensteln, W.
H. Thompson, Paul Keyser, Charles Leist
1 and H. Hopkins.
The penalty is six months' imprison
ment, $r,OO tine, or both, and Judge Gra
ham will pass sentence next Wednesday
unless an. appeal is taken. The immediate
I result of the conviction, it is believed, will
| be the closing of the "cribs" by Chief
Lees, and if this is done those who have
labored so hard to accomplish this end
will feel that their labors have not been
i in vain. The fact that Director Richards
I was not convicted with the rest of the
gang was due to the fact that he could
I not be found by the police.
The evidence adduced at the trial
I showed a deplorable condition of moral
! depravity and the lengths to which some
creatures In the guise of men will go in
order to gain a few filthy dollars. Attor- I
| ney Joseph Dunne, who represented the
defendants, sought to show that his
clients did not rent the premises to the
- unfortunate outcasts who occupy the
I cribs for a few hours each day or night,
but this contention was speedily shown
to be false by their own witnesses under
the rigid and skillful cross-examination
I of Attorney ,T. J. Barrett, who prosecuted
; the case for the North Beach residents.
Blumenberg swore that the Nymphia
i whs leased to a woman known as Lillie
Dale, and the latter took the stand in
support of this assertion, but her testi
mony alone was sufficient to convict the
defendants she was so anxious to shield
from the consequences of their shameless
greed. She swore that she rented the
place for $'.00 a month; that the "hotel"
contained ninety rooms, or cribs, and that
since July 1 of this year the largest num
i ber that were unoccupied on any one day
was forty-seven, and the smallest num
[ ber was thirty. From each occupant she
1 received $3 a day, and the monthly reve
nue amounted to about $4000. This money
i she claimed she kept In her husband's
I safe In his saloon across the street, but
! on cross-examination she admitted that I
i she had not paid any rent, and that there i
was not a thousand dollars In the safe.
1 Of course the defendants expected the
| Jury to believe that they leased the place
to the woman without insisting on the
payment of the rent or without an ac
counting of any sort.
F. Venture was one of the principal
witnesses for the prosecution, and he tes
tified that Blumenberg In soliciting him
1 to buy stock in the Twinkling Star Com
'< pany told him that his scheme was to
i have every fallen woman in the city
; take rooms in the "hotel." It was shown
; that the women who rent the cribs are
I the exiles from Qulncy alley. Bacon piace
! and St. Mary's place, and they were in
! the habit of occupying the cribs but a
few hours during the day or night. They
; had their homes elsewhere. Lieutenant
I of Police Martin and Officer Clark, who
I had been detailed on the case by Chief
I Lees to assist Mr. Barrett, also gave
1 damaging testimony against the defend
ants. The Jury was out less than fifteen
minutes. Judge Graham announced that
he would pass sentence next Wednesday.
The Tight against the Hotel Xymphla
has been waged ever since the scheme to
1 colonize immorality and vice In the neigh
borhood of Stockton and Pacific streets
was rt'-st given publicity. As originally
planned the ?pcon<l Moor of the main
building was intended for a big saloon,
but Attorney Barrett, acting on behalf of
thÂ» residents In th- vicinity, went before
I the Police Commissioners nnd success
fully opposed the application for a licence.
Mr. Barrett also went before the Bcurd
i of Supervisor* when a petition was pre- |
I sented by the Twinkling Star Improve- I
ment Company asking to be- allowed to !
I alter the basement of the building facing
! or. Stockton street, which was to form I
! the west win? of the "hotel," and secured
the nonage of Â« resolution declaring the
| place ft public nuisance. '

JUNIOR FORESTERS.
A New Court Instituted Last Night,
the First in Fifteen Years.
Tuesday night for the first time in fifteen
years there was organized in this city a
new Junior Court of Forester* of Amer
ica. The organization took place in the
Alcazar building, aim the institution
which followed the preliminary meeting
was by Grand Sub-chie^ Ranger Hugo K.
Asher, Grand Secretary Jno. J. Corny,
Eddie Isaacs and H. Simons, assisted by
a number of members of adult and other
junior courts. The new coi'ft, which Is
under the protection of Court Bay City,
starts in with thirty-one charter members,
and the following named were chosen as
officers for the current term: Jacob La
bel J. P. C. R.; 1. Levy, C. R.; A. Sam
uels, S. C. R.; I. O. Kasser, F. B.j O. G.
Day. R. S. : J. . Kallmann. T. : Charles
David, S. A.; K. Mutinoff. J. A.; E. Ghise
ler, S. R. and J. A. Magldson. J. B.
A. NusÃbaum is the physician and
Thomas Elliott. L. Aaron and I. David
trustees. Thomas Goodwin was recom
mended for deputy grand chief . ranger.
After the Installation of the officers Chief
Ranger Levy presided as toastmaster at a
supper given in honor of the new court.
Hugo K. Asher responded to "The For
esters of America." Harry Simons to the
"Junior Courts." Jno. J. Cordv to the
"Grand Court of California." I. Isaacs to
"Sister Junior Courts." John Heenan to
"The Ladies." Jacob Label to "The For
ester Press," O. G. Day to "Junior Court
Pride of the Pacific" and Thomas Good
win to "The Supreme Court of Forest
ers." The new court will be known as
"Junior Court Pride of the Pacitic No. 4."

Ruby Jttayinond Held.
Ruby Raymond, the woman from San
Jose charged with abducting two girls,

under 18 years of age, to San Jose for im
moral purposes, was yesterday held to

MAY FORM A
NEW CLUB OF
WHEELMEN
Recruits Expected From the
Ranks of the Olympics
and Bay Citys.
The recent announcement of the new
Jirectorate of the Olympic Club that the ,
monthly dues of the organization would ,
be raised from $.". to $5 has met with some ,
opposition among the rank and file of the .
club, and particularly among the mem- j
bers of the cycling annex. The wheel- j
men are all young men, and even now .ire â  ,
paying about $1 50 a month more than the ,
members of any other cycling club, the
next highest being the Bay City Whe-M- j
men, whose dues are 35 a quarter. In a I- ;
ditlon to the $3 dues, the wheelmen i iy '
21 cents a month additional :.s members
of the annex. This makes a total of $'.'j'j ,
a year, a considerable item In the ex
penses of many of the young men.
An px-presidem of the wheelmen, who ,
ha â  also Ueeti president of the California I
Associated Cycling clubs, crystallised the I
feelings of.'a large number of the mem
bers, when he said in an Informal dis
cussion after the association's meeti'ig ;
Monday night: "On the day I receive a;
notice stating the dues are to be $5 a |
month. 1 shall indorse my resignation on :
the back and return it to the club. I can- \
not afford to pay &i 25 a month to belong .
to a wheeling organization, for, as a mat- |
ter of fact, 1 derive no other beneilta i
from the Olympic Club, because I do not I
have time to go there." The speaker is
probably better able to pay the sum men
tioned than dozens of others In the ;
cycling annex.
Speaking further, he said: "My idea
is that If they raise the dues, we will get j
about fifty representative wheelmen to- j
gether arid organize another club, with !
down-town headquarters similar to those
of the Olympic Gun Club. 1 know that
a number of the old-time Bay Citys are i
dissatisfied with their club just now,, be- '
catipe if the recent large influx of small i
boys into the club, some of whom have
been given places on the board of di- ;
rectors. No doubt they are excellent fel- '
lows, but the men In the Bay Cltya aged
from 28. say, to 3T>. gain nothing mentally
or otherwise by association with boys of [
18 or 20. I believe we could get a score i
of the better. class of old members into;
our new organization. Don't be surprised ;
if you hear of a new club about the â 
middle of October, with an age limit of
25 and dues about $2 or $2 50 a month,
which will cater to gentlemen cyclers
only."
John A. Hammersmith, leader of the i
Olvmplc Club, when Informed of a pos- \
sible defection of members said: "The
new board of directors of the club have
given Its financial condition moat careful
consideration, it has been found that â¢
nothing but heroic measures can save it. .
The club cannot be supported on the ba
sis of dues at $2 50 a month. The mom- i
bers of the board have therefore decided
to increase the dues to $5 on the Ist of
October. We expect to lose a few mem- I
bers, but the increased revenue and the j
new members we wjll receive will more
than compensate. "We will offer many ad- j
vantages to the members of the club
which no other organization could hope
to give its members."
Clarence N. Ravlin. formerly a well
known writer on cycle topics hero, has re
turned from a six months' trip to the
mining regions of Alaska. He says his
prospects are excellent, and he expects to I
return north in about four months.
Jules P. Hancock, formerly treasurer of
the Associated Clubs, was married at St. i
Rose's Church last Sunday to Miss Adah
Averlll of Nevada. They are enjoying a
honeymoon tandem trip in Lake County.
Joseph A. Ostendorf of the Bay City !
Wheelmen, now a resident of Los Angeles,
writes his friends here that he is the
happy father of twins, both bays. The
club "members have wired him their con r ,
gratulations.

HANDICAP
WEIGHTS AND
ENTRIES
Brief Review of Some Likely
Starters in the Big Event
Saturday.
The opening handicapâ a mile, worth
$700 to the winnerâ the special feature of
the racing card on Saturday at Oakland
track, will Bee about as evenly a balanced
field as has yet faced the barrier in this
rich event, The entries and weights were
Issued by Handicapper Brooks yesterday;
and on paper the race looks an extremely
difficult one to pick. On his Sacramento
form Cromwell has rightfully been al
lotted top weight, 117 pounds. During the
State Fair meet the chestnut horse seemed
to have recovered his old-time speed, and
will be hard to beat. Mt. McGregor II
comes next with 113, and Roslnante looks
"well In" with 109 up. The latter mare
has had a long rest and will take a lot
of beating. At Sacramento, Hohenzollern
was both good and bad, and with 108
pounds on his back will be dangerous.
Jennie Reed is greatly improved, and So
cialist has been going great guns at Oak
land in his work. Libertine, no longer
partial to weight, should be able to reel
off a very fast mile with only ninety
eight pounds in the saddle, and Lost Girl,
allotted ninety-eight pounds also, will not
want for backers. On September I,\ at
Oakland track, Lothian downed his field
over one mile and a sixteenth, galloping
In 1:49%, carrying eighty-eight pounds,
and asked to carry one pound more on
Saturday,. has an undeniable chance. The
other lightweights should not be over
looked. The full list of entries are as fol
lows:

ing at Oakland, and it Is probable that a
Starting device other than the Australian
gate will be given a trial.

In 1894, will arrive on the next, incoming
Steamer from Australia. He is a son of
the renowned sire Xewmtnster, and is
consigned to Senator It. Porter Ashe.
GREYHOUNDS AND THEIR
TRAINERS A NUISANCE
Since hare hunting and the breeding
and training of greyhounds have become
a sport the residents of the Richmond
district have been sorely annoyed by the
men having the training of the dogs in
charge. Point LobOÃ avenue by reason
of its soft and spongy surface, together
with the wide range of territory In which
free access is afforded for the sprinting
of the greyhounds, has become a favorite
rendezvous for all the dog trainers in the
city. If the dog trainers had confined
their avocation to the sprinting of the
dogs in a legitimate sort of a way the
f>eople would not have grumbled, but of
ate the trainers have taught the dogs to
run down and kill cats, dogs and chickens
on sight.
This nuisance has become so great that
the people residing in the Richmond dis
trict have petitioned the Chief of Police
to detail a mounted officer to patrol the
district and try to keep the dogs and
their owners and trainers within legiti
mate bounds.
An Incident which at one time bid fair
to result in something more serious than
tbe loss of a cat's life took place on Point
Lobos avenue, where a very valuable pet
dog, belonging to Mrs. Mary A. McCabe,
residing. at 2yf>o Point Lobos avenue, was
killed by a pair of greyhounds out on a
training expedition. The fleet footed dogs
espied the little pet on the sidewalk in
front of Its mistress' house and gave
chase, which resulted In the killing of
the small dog. Mrs. McCabe was very
angry at the loss of her pet, and seeing
no sorrow on the part of the trainers, she
secured a pistol and blazed away at the
greyhounds, wounding one severely, her
only regret being that she did not suc
ceed In killing the two hounds. The shoot
ing at the dogs created quite a commo
tion in the neighborhood, but when the
cause was explained to those who con
gregated they all sympathized with Mrs.
McCabe.
The dog training nuisance has become
so great that the residents have sought
the assistance of the Point L.obos Im
provement Club to aid them in having
the nuisance abated. At the last meet
ing of the club the secretary was in
structed to communicate with the Chief
of Police on the subject.'
THE SAS FKAIN CISCO CAUj, THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 21, 1899.
effort for possession of the America's cup could not be made by a worthier
boat nor a more sportsmanly challenger.
The Shamrock's splendid performance on her first trial in American
â waters is as gratifying to yachtsmen of this country as it is no doubt to j
those of Great Britain. Too long our defense of this intfrnational trophy |
has partaken of the nature of a sail-over. So easy has been the conquest,
indeed, a majority of the times it has failed to provide that excitement
without which a sport speedily loses vital interest.
Many American sportsmen would welcome for several good reasons
loss of the cup this year; chief of them is the tremendous stimulation that
would be given yachting interests in England and in Americaâ for a chal
lenge would be issued forthwith In the name of the New York Yacht Club
and several boats built in both countries for the race. It is not for the j
best interests of any sport that one country or one college or one club
should always win; really in the end such one-sided competition affects
the sporting development of the winner as much as it does that of the j
loser.
â¢ â¢ â¢
When the next challenge is issued we hope it will be with a seventy
and not with a ninety footer. Apropos of which an excellent story Is

The most impressive feature of the Shamrock's sailing off Sandy Hook
last week was tho comparatively slight disturbance she made in going
through the water. Evidently there is something in her underbody which
Kives good warrant for the confidence of Sir Thomas and for William
Fife's insistence that she be launched in skirts. Certainly the Shamrock's
performance the other day brought unfeigned surprise to those who looked
upon her for the first time, or had perhaps seen photographs of her under
.sail in the Solent.
There is no doubt the Shamrock was expected to make quite a fuss as
she went through the water. Reports of her work on the other side spoke
of it, and her full bow and heavy quarters suggested it.
But nothing of the kind was visible in her sailing last week. On the
contrary, she went through the water smoothly and with astonishing
ease. Instead of pounding with her full bow and sending the spray high

ALL TEAMS
ARE PLAYING
GOOD GAMES
Changes in the Last Week
Which Bring Nines Closer
in the Column.
The four teams In the league to-day
are approaching a common level of game
with startling rapidity. Three of them
are in the same hundred percentage with
the Dudes still trailing a bad fourth.
The position of Cal Ewing's men is un
fortunate. At the beginning of the sea
son his men played very poor ball and
they made speedy descents down the col
umn which represents the standing of the
teams. As it is now they are 166 per cent
below the leading Senators and nearly
a century behind the Lobsters, who stand
third in a list of four teams. But the
playing of the Dudes of late is not that
of tail enders. It is really equal to the
best and when Sacramento crosses bats
with the Oakland team it will find a much
fortified nine to bat Into subjection. The
Dudes took the measure of the locals in
two out of three games last Saturday and
Sunday by playing the better ball. That
they lost the first game was a matter of
The local team has lost Its winning
stride and in consequence has gone down
the percentage column with a hop. skip
and a jump. The two victories of Santa
Cruz over the Senators have advanced the
Lobsters several stations, and they will
light San Francisco for second place un
less that team regains its old-time form.
The standing of the teams is as fol
lows:

The rivalry existing between teams has
broken out in fistic demonstrations and
this is something which the managers
should use every means within their
power to guard against. The squabble
between Dunleavy and Hildebrand, wh,ile
a personal affair, places their team in
bad odor. One redeeming feature of the
encounter is that it was not on the field.
Managers Harris and Kwing are alive to
the disposition of their men and they
should fine all players heavily who inter
fere with the progress of the game dur
ing contests, when no good reason for
protracted recalcitrancy exists, or when
their men engage in conduct that is hood
lumlike in character.

CLOSE-HAULED ON STARBOARD TACK.
over her forward dock she rides over the water like a shallow-draught
center-boarder, and does less hammering than any ninety-footer that has
ever come out. of England.
She seems, indeed, to make less disturbance at her bow in a choppy
sea than the Columbia, although the latter has not been seen yet in such
weatherly conditions as those which prevailed during the challenger's first
sail on this side.
Somehow the Shamrock appears to displace less water at the bow than
the Columbia; nor docs water so much boil up on each side of the yacht,
to swirl along her side and under her counter for the making of a consid
erable wake, as Is the case with the defender.
â¢ * â¢
It is difficult, perhaps impossible, to make trustworthy comparisons
without the yachts sailing side by side in the same water and air, but
carrying in the mind's eye the work of the Columbia under somewhat
similar conditions it docs look as though Fife had succeeded in giving his
product an easier entrance, for all its seemingly heavy bow, than Herres
hoff has secured for the more finely drawn racerlike bow of the Columbia.
And the value of easy entrance cannot be overestimated. It might
mean minutes in a thirty-eight mile race over a choppy cross-sea, and it
surely means seconds under any conditions.
Yet it must be borne in mind that no one has viewed the Columbia
under such conditions, or with the rig she was designed to carry, in
weather approaching that the Shamrock encountered on her second sail.

COLUMBIA
FAVORITE IN
THE BETTING
Odds of 10 to 8 Are Being
Offered on the American
Yacht
SDecial Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Sept. 20.â Betting on the
America's cup match has not yet become
active. Now and then wagers are heard
of on the result, but the amounts have
i been small. Before the arrival of the
! Shamrock the inclination was to make
the Columbia a great favorite, and there
were rumors of money being posted at
odds of two and three to one on the home
boat, but it was impossible at the time
to find out who the enthusiastic patriots
were that felt so sanguine that the Co
lumbia would win.
The Columbia still has the call In the
; betting, but it is conceded that the Sham-
I rock is a better boat than any of the pre
! vious challengers. The disposition on the
I part of the Columbia's admirers seems to>
I be to wait for terms not one-sided. Oc
casional bets have recently been made of
10 to S and even 10 to 7, with the Columbia
favorite, but the amounts have not been
large. The bulk of the Columbia money
will not be bet until next week, when the
holders of large sums hope to obtain even
money, but failing in this a point or two
may be conceded to the Shamrock ad
herents.
A prominent member of a city club .lust
returned from London reports that he suc
ceeded in placing Â£200 on the Columbia
there at even money and wanted more,
but was unable to get It. At the time he
made the bet there was only a meager
amount of speculation on the result of
the races.

pecially fine.CALIFORNIANS
WILL DOFF THE
BLUE TO-DAY
Both Commands to Be
Mustered Out.
COLORS WILL BE RETAINED
THE TWENTY-SEVENTH HAS
SAILED AWAY.
probable Chang-e of Programme for
the Grant and the Thirtieth
Regiment â No More
Smallpox Reported.
By to-night the California Volunteers
"will be out of service. The artillery will
go rst, and then the regiment will be
raid off and discharged. There win be no
ceremony about the discharge, and Its
most prominent feature Will be the clink
of gold. Not that the California men will
get very much in the way of money, but
it will be the first paid over to the men In
a long time. The artillery battalion has
had a pay-day since It returned here, but
It Is some months since the men of the
First Regiment have received anything.
They will get no travel pay. for the men
all enlisted here, and although there is
something due on the clothing allowances
of most of the men, it will not be much.
Before the regiment has gone out of the
service Colonel Duboce will issue a last
order to his men. In it he will recall to
their minds their old service in the guard,
and â  â â¢â¢ will ask that the men stay to
gether, re-enter the service of the State,
and perpetuate the name and fame and
the organization of the old First Regi
ment.
The regtmental colors will be retained
as the sacred trust of the reorganized
regiment. There was a plan on Coot to
have them placed on -exhibition in the
rotunda of the City Hall, and kept there
us one of the treasures of the city, but
Colonel Duboce declined to let them go.
He did not feel justified in giving them
up he uttid, as the old guard regiment was
to be reformed, and he, and his officers,
believed the -colors should be retained by
those who had added to their luster.
They will be carried in all parades, in ad
uition to the new colors the regiment will
get, â¢ and a special guard of honor,
selected from among the men who fought
under â  them, will escort them. Their
place will be in the regimental armory.
.Battery D of the Volunteer Artillery
will leave for Los Angeles to-morrow by
s-peeial train. The men are all from the
touth, most of them from Los Angeles,
and a royal reception is awaiting them
down there.
Some of the men inarched from the
Presidio, and the remainder were
brought around on the tug Sea King.
The transport Tacoma, which was to
have sailed at 6 o'clock last evening, was
obtained until this morning on account
of a defective refrigerator. The George
W. Elder left her lock and anchored off
the Large office at 4 p. m. yesterday. She
bailed early this morning. The Twenty
seventh Regiment was distributed be
tween the two transports.
Several small boys tried to stow away on
the Elder, but they were discovered and
sent to the Harbor police station by the
uincers of the transport service. Boys 10
12 and 13 years of age seemed to nave
acquired the craze for going to the Phil
ippines, and every time a transport la
about to sail there are half a dozen moth
ers down at the Government wharf look
ing for missing sons.
The Twenty-seventh has left Just in
time to make room for the Thirty-second, ;
which will reach the Presidio to-day from
Fort X-eavenworth. Kansas. The Thirty
second is in command of Colonel Louis A.
raig.
The -.ran sport Grant will probably take
away the Thirtieth Regiment. She will
coma In to the Government wharf to-day
and the supplies for the Thirty-first that
are aboard will be unloaded. The Thir
tieth Regiment was to have sailed on the
Sherman, but as that vessel has been de
layed by the boiler-makers' strike and the
Grant is ready for sea the men will go on
her.
The hospital ship Relief has been de
layed again. She was to have sailed yes

JUDGE COFFEY
IS PREJUDICED
Laura James Contest
in Court.
TWO AFFIDAVITS ARE FILED
ATTORNEYS HART AND FOX THE
AFFIANTS.
Both Contend That a Decision Ad
verse to Their Client Was
Reached Through Failure
to Credit Witnesses.
Judge Coffev has again teen charged
with prejudice. Two affidavits making
! this periodical charge againb: his Honor
, were filed yesterday morning by .W. if.
; H. Hart of Blythe case fame and George
j W. Fox, who is associated with Hart in
prosecuting the Interests of Laura Milan
James, the alleged contract widow of the
late Dr. C. A. James, and her infant son,
I Theodore Milan James.
The- charge of prejudice against Judge
I Coffey is the outcome of certain decisions
handed down by the court declaring- the
claims of the .alleged widow without foun
'â  dation.. Dr. James died a few years ago
i at his Howard street residence, leaving a
'large estate. As he died intestate the
property was tinder course of admiliistra
! lion pending distribution to relatives in
1 Indiana, when Mrs. James came forward
and tiled a petition in which she sought
distribute to her of all the estate by
virtue of her alleged relationship.
The case was assigned to Judge Con>y
I for trial, the marriage contract was pro
| duced and evidence to substantiate airs.
James' claim Introduced. Considerable
evidence tending to prove that the signa
ture on the contract was genuine was in
-1 troduced, but Juage Coffej handed down
I a decision declaring the document to be a
forgery. An appeal was taken to the Su
preme Court and the decision reversed,
! the higher tribunal holding that the lower
court erred In admitting certain evidence
' and excluding other.
Now that the case is back before the
Superior Court the attorneys named ob-
I ject to another trial before Judge Coffey,
i both claiming that the court was preju
diced and decided the case against nts
I own opinion. In his affidavit Attorney !
â¢ Fox contends that Judge Coffey. compared
' signatures himself and failed to consider
, the evidence given by experts who j
testified that the one on the mar
riage contract was genuine, the
evidence pi Expert R. C. Hop
j kins, whose ability has been recognized
| by Circuit Judge Sawyer and other dis
tinguished . jurists, being among that
which the court failed to consider.
I Hart reached further into the case in
; his affidavit than does Fox. He says
that he has known Judge Coffey for many
years and that he Is positive that this j
jurist is a man of set opinions and that I
I in passing upon documents similar to the ;
one in question his Honor frequently does,
and especially in this case did. pass upon
the genuineness of the signature from his j
I own opinion and discredited the opinion of j
more experienced persons. He further
Bets forth that the action of Judge Coffey
in arriving at this adverse decision from i
his own opolnions closed an avenue of ac
tion, as "counsel were deprived of the
benefit as well as the pleasure of cross- :
examining his Honor on the subject of |
handwriting." For ttifse reasons Hart is (
confident that Judge Coffey cannot retry
the case without entertaining his opinion
heretofore formed, and hence a transfer
to another department is prayed for.
It is understood that when the motion i '
for a transfer comes up this morning At
torney Shadbourne. who represents the
heirs, will file a counter-affidavit, and the ; ,
contest between counsel, spiced now am!
then by the remark? of the court, will
make an interesting hearing.

Charge of Embezzlement Against the
Proprietor of the Ahlborn
House.
H. .T. Renner. a visitor from Georgia,
swore to a complaint in Judge Conlan's
court yesterday for the arrest of August
Fehlemelcher, proprietor of the Ahlborn
House, on the charge of felony embezzle
ment, and Fehlemelcher was arrested and
booked on the charge. He was released
on bonds.
Renner stated that when he went to the
Ahlborn House as a guest he deposited
with the proprietor a roll of bills amount
ing to $640. When he asked for the bills
yesterday the defendant's wife took: the
j-.ill out of the safe, but on counting it he
found that $140 was missing. He demand
balance, but w.a.- refused, and as
certained later that the proprietor had
lost the money in gambling.
Fehlemelcher admits that Renner
handed him a mil of bills, and when he
offered to count it he told him it was
all right. The roll was pirn-â¢} in the safe
and returned to Renner by defendant's
â¢wife. Two hour? later Renner came back
and said there was $140 short. The nll
had not b'-eu interfered with from the

livered to Rentier.

The Oceanic Steamship Company's
mail steamer Australia sailed for Hono
lulu yesterday with sixty-five cabli: and
forty steerage pessengers and over 1000
tons of cargo. This is an unusually heavy
load of merchandise, hut 8000 tons more
would have been shipped could the vessel
have carried it.
The. coal passers and firemen on the
Australia went out happy. A few hours
before ih< vessel sailed they sent a dele
gation t" Captain Howard, superintend
ent of the company, asking for an ad
vance of wages. The matter was referred
to the head office, and an advance of $."'
a month w:is given the men. The w;tKÂ«-s
for firemen and coal passers i? now back
to what ir was flve years ago, and the
men arc in mn?. quence juhllant.
It is now believed that the big pile raft
which broke away from the Czarina has
gone to pieces. The schooner Defender
reports passing through a number of
plies with the nark on, and the Cleone,
when two miles r.ff Caspar, saw two lots
Of them. The raft that was towed in hy
the tugs Rnll^f and Rescue was com
posed of barked piles. A number of them
drifted out during the tow and have since
been spoken. The raft which got away
from thi- Czarina was composed of piles
with the bark on, and it '.vaa probably
portions of it that the Defender and
Cleone saw.
The Cleone towed the schooner Dewey
down from Albion and narrowly escaped
colliding with an unknown collier. The
captain of the Cleone says he signaled
that he had a tow und the collier
answered. Instead of looking out for the
tow the collier cut across the Cleone'9
stern und struck the Dewey a glancing
blow. No damage was done, and both
vessels held on their course.

and will go East in a few days. The
lieutenant is a son of the late Admiral
David E. Porter.

RECEIVED THEIR BOYS.
Entertainment for the Men
Who Enlisted From St.
Joseph's Parish.
There was a complimentary reception
to the members of the two California vol
unteer commands at St. Joseph's Hall
last evening. The reception was in honor
of those members of the regiment and
the artillery battalion who are members
of the parish of St. Joseph, but there
was no limit to the soldiers who were
to be received. Every one who wore the j
blue was welcome and every one of the
men who came was made to feel him
self so.
A most interesting and entertaining
programme had been prepared, and when
it opened there was not a vacant seat
in the hall. On the platform were some
of the prominent people of the parish and
several of the officers- of the regiment,
and through the audience the bluecoats
were scattered from stage to doorway.
The feature of the programme was an
address by Father McKinnon, the chap
lain of the First, who wove Into a sim- j
ple tale of the life of the men In the I
islands a good deal of information about i
affairs down there. What the soldier
needs, he said, is not criticism, but sup- |
port; he needs the encouragement of his
people at home; he needs more evidences
of patriotism and less fault (hiding. It
Is easy to* tell how to run things down
there, he said, but it Is not so easy to go
down and run them, and In that con
nection he paid a compliment to General
Otis, who, he said, is as prudent and
hard working a man as there is in the
army.
In speaking of the people Father Mc-
Kinnon said there are eighteen tribes, all
favorably disposed toward the United
States except the Tagailos. The Tagal
lOÃ, he said, are treacherous, and as an ;
instance of their treachery he told how ;
they once went into the northern part |
of the island and inviting all the tribes
in the vicinity to meet them they asked
; who was in favor of peace. A vote was
taken and a great many declared them
selves for peace, and the next day the
Tagallos sought them out and killed them
all. The war will last, he said, until there !
are troops enough In the islands to pro- i
tect those who are kept from showing
their allegiance to this country through
fear of the Tagallos. When they are pro- |
tected they will come out as friends and j
then the Tagallos will be left alone and
cannot hold out. There are 50,000 of them
armed with modern arms.
Father McKinnon spoke enthusiastically
of the work of the men from California
and he praised the work of General
Smith, particularly his government of
Negros.
When Father McKinnon came upon the
stage he was given an ovation that few
men can claim. Everyone roÃe and every- '
one shouted, and for some minutes there ;
v.-as no stopping the storm. His remarks
: throughout wen greeted with bursts of \
applause, particularly the persona] inci
dents he told of and the deeds of bravery
he described.
The audience was welcomed by Father
Scanlan, pastor of St. Joseph's church.
The reception whs under the auspices of
the St. Vincent de Paul Society of the I
parish. The programme in full was as ;
. follows: :-!*'

king bnnjo; T. McMahon. accordion; Introduc
tory remark, John T. Dowlln*, president St.
Vincent & Paul Society; address of welcome,
Rev Father Bcaltlan, pastor of St. Joseph's;
Church; ballad. Miss Lattte Crawford; cornet <

GRATIA VANCE
HAD A TRIO
OF HUSBANDS
Judge Coffey Hears a
Unique Contest.
SEEKING A CHILD'S CUSTODY
SON OF THE DECEASED IS THE
OBJECT OF LITIGATION.
The Court Must Determine the Pa
rentage of the Child as Well as
the I/=gal Stc.tus of the
Case at Bar.
The contest for the guardianship of Ira
Leroy Vance, chiid of the late Gratia
Vance, was commenced before Judge Cof
fey yesterday, and seme interesting sec
tions of tlie family skeleton of the de
ceased thai have long been closeted were
dragged into i> ibitc view. Ira Lincoln
Vance, who adopted the boy In question,
seeks his custody by virtue of the re
lationship he believes he bean toward
the boy. while Julia Evans Rose, the
youth's grandmother, prays for his cus
tody, claiming that by reason of her
daughters many and peculiar marriages
fhe boy's alfeged father is not entitled
to his custody.
Gratia Vance, mother of the lad whose
fate is now banging in the balance, died
a short time ago leeaving an estate of
some little value. Ira Lincoln Vance
Immediately made application for letters
.of administration upon the .'state and
also asked for the custody of the child.
Within a few "lays Mrs. Rose filed a sim
ilar application, and the contest was on.
Yesterday it went to trial, and through
the testimony runs the thread of an In
teresting tale.
According to the testimony introduced
by Mrs. Hose, her daughter, several years
ago In a little town in New York, married
Frederick Dunne. They lived together for
a short time, and then, for some unknown
reasons drifted apart. Next, without hay.
ing been divorced from Dunne, she be
came the wfe of Orson Debillo Hill.
Finally she drifted away from Hill, but
while her marriage to him was still in
full force and effect she again went
through the ceremony of marriage witn
the petitioner, Ira Lincoln Vance.
It is said that the child Is the natural
son of the second husband. Hill, but if,
as asserted, when she married Hill the
Child's mother was the legal wite of
Dunne, the lad is of illegitimate parent
age. Hill denies that she was the wile
of Dunne when he manod her. He fle-
Clares she wns divorced and that the child
is his legitimate son. Vance claims other
wise however. He says the woman who
bore his name at the time of her aeath
was his legal wife for the reason that
Dunne was dead when he married her,
and that the fact that Dunne was alive
when Hill married her invalidated Hills
marriage, as there was no divorce, in
consequence, he declares he is entitled to
letters of administration and the guarrt
lanship of the child, which, he says, he
adopted, with his mother's consent,
shortly after he married her.
Hill 'has nominated Mrs. Hose as guard
ian of the child, claiming that as the nat
ural father of the child he has the ngat
to dictate as to matters concerning its
future welfare. After Mrs. Rose had
finished her testimony an adjournment
was taken.

was caused by the interference of one
Gottfried TeuFcher. and advised that

erated and they would find domestic con
tent.

SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver and Exchange about the same.
Wheat still dull, but rather stronger everywltere.
Barley, Oats and Corn neglected.
Flour and Millstuffs as previously quoted. ; ' i
Beam and Seeds dull and unchanged.
Hay in lessened receipt and rather firmer for choice.
Potatoes, Onions and I 'egctahlcs about the same.
Xo variation in Poultry worthy of note.
Fancy Butter firm and other grades weak.
Cheese and Eggs quiet and not over steady.
Wine Crapes continue to advance under scarcity.
Another rise in Melons. Lemons and Limes scarce.
Pried Fruits in good demand.
Xo change in Provisions.

The Hay Market.
The circular of SonWTS & Co. says of Hay:
"Receipts for the week ending to-day show
a total somewhat less than for the week pre
ceding, the account as Indicated about amount
ing to about 4000 tons, In comparison with 4300
tons for the week preceding. The situation 1?
practically uncharged here, although the ab
sence of strictly choice wheat hay Is more
marked now than at any time previous this
season. There is plenty of gm>d hay coming.
but for retail purposes we must have gilt-edge
â¢wheat hay and choice . ill! .- red or black tame
oat. These two varieties will probably be in
fair demand throughout the entire year, but j
there Is such an Immense amount of other j
grades that for the present at least there
seems to be a very discouraging future for
same.
"We are continually hearing of large lots of
tally hearing of larse lots of
hay that are being used In private store
houses and in many warehouse buildings where i
the owners are not considering for a moment :
marketing their hay under present prices. It j
(<oe., seen at present from the number of new- .
barns erected that we will have a large sur
plus of hay at the end of the present season un
)r-ss. as we have expressed slves before,
eomo unexpected demand springs up."

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure has risen rapidly over the
northern half of the Pacific- Slope. An area
of high pressure Is moving In from the Pacific
and causing generally cloudy and much colder
weather over thÂ» northern half of the Pacific
flone It will probably be followed by rain
over the northern half of the coast Thursday
The temperature has fallen slightly over Cal
ifornia. Nevada. T'tnh and Arizona. At Port- !
land there has been a fall of 20 degrees and at
Seattle IS degrees during the past twenty-four
hours. Warm weather prevails In the valleys
of California. Conditions have been favorable
for raisin making near Fresno. A maximum
temperature of 92 degrees is reported at Mount
Tamalpals rid a corresponding maximum of
12 at Point Reyes.
Forecast made at Pan Francisco for thirty
hours ling mldnlgl September 21, 1899:
For Northern Californiaâ Cloudy Thursday;
probably light showers in extreme northern
portion Thursday night; cooler In the interior;
western winds on the coast, with fog.
For Southern Californiaâ Fair Thursday, with
fog along the coast: light westerly winds.
For Nevadaâ Fair Thursday; cooler in the
.northern portion.
For Utahâ Cloudy; cooler Thursday. â¢

EASTERN MARKETS.
New York Stock Market.
XCT' YORK, Sept. 20.â This was a day of
violent fluctuations and great speculative ex
citement upon the Stock Exchange. The move
ment of prices . .... bo feverish and uncertain
that the disposition among professional opera
tors was generally to close up accounts with
tho day. As a consequence net changes are
astonishingly email considering the wide vi
l.ratiun during the day. in only a few instances
do they exceed fractions, and though the ma
jority of Kti.cka are lower there is a fair sprink
ling of gains. There was a time in the morn
ing when a long 111 â¢ of specialties had advanced
between 1% and 3 points and during the after
noon they' Cell very generally to as much be
low last night's level. A range of between
S and S points was traversed during the day
by the lowing Indus-trial Block*:
Continental â¢ Tobacco, â  Smelling preferred,
Colorado Fuel. Consolidated (Jan. Federal Steel,
Manhattan, People's Gun, Tobacco, Metropoli
tan Strtet Railway and Tennessee Coal. Ihe
railroad stocks did not move nearly so widely
aH a rule, but an extreme range of between
Ivs and 4>* wtis traversed by St. Paul, Rock
Inland. Burlington, Northwestern, Great west
ern "A." Chicago and Eastern Illinois, St.
Paul ..... Duluth, Great Northern preferred,
leading first preferred and Lackawanna. ThÂ»
index of the market all day was Brooklyn
Transit, the dealings in which were on an
enormous scale. With a total capitalization of
430.000 shares, to-day's dealings In this stock
footed up 285,175 shares. it advanced 2% after
the opening, fell VA to a new low level at 704.
and then retraced its course, rising 8 points
to 83Vs. and closing at the high level of the
morning. The whole market followed in its
wake In a general way, though Its erratic
fluctuations were impossible to follow at all
times. The market was a gigantic speculative
contest throughout. The bears broke down the
(rains which had been built up in the morning
with apparently little effort and dislodged some
very heavy offerings on the decline from hold
ers who had vainly hoped that the fall in
prices bad come to an end. There was large
selling through commission houses and tha
flood of industrials poured upon the market
â¢was said to be due to the calling of some large
loans in which these stocks figured as collater
als and had to be sold to liquidate. United
States . Milling, which sold yesterday at 56,
Bold to-day at 24, after first selling at 2.'.. an
extreme decline of 32 points. The preferred
Etock, after selling yesterday at 75, sold to-day
at 80, The closing prices for these stocks were
for the common 20tf}25 and for the preferred
40050. There was no Important development
to-day 'in the ' money situation. The rise In
New York exchange at Chicago and the ad
vance'in the call loans at- some other Eastern
cities' indicates that the Western demand for
money has been shifted some from New York
to the other Eastern cities. Considerable sums
of Chicago money are reported to have been
loaned in New York during the week. The
outgo, however, still continues both to the in
terior and to the sub-treasury. Foreign ex
change was somewhat easy, more notably bo
on Paris and Berlin than on London. At this
time last year when New York's surplus re
nerve had fallen below {5, GOO gold began
promptly to come In from Europe. Previous
to September last year we had already im
ported over $100,000,000 of gold on balance.
â while thus far this year the excess of ex
ports of gold has been nearly $3,000,000. Al
though the merchandise exports for eight
months of this year Is nearly $75,000,000 less
than last year the unliquidated trade balance
in our favor thus ' far this year is therefore
jo ; , 000,000 greater than for the same period last
year. Sterling exchange Is still IV4 cents In
the pound above the level at which gold Im
ports began last year.
Bonds weakened In sympathy with stocks,
but the movement was not wide. Total sales
par value, $1. 512.000.
United States bonds were unchanged In bid
quotations.

Boston Wool Market.
BOSTON, Sept. 20.â The American Wool and
â¢"ott.in Reporter will say to-morrow:
' Another large week's business has been
transacted in the wool market, the sales for
the week aggregating over 9,000,000 pounds.
The market has. In fact, been somewhat ex
cited at times.
T*io weeks aeo some of the sales effected
were reported to have been made slightly
under the edge of the market, but the trans
actions of the past week certainly have been
in most cases at full quotations. Strength
ened by the opening at the London sales,
where prices were quoted 10Â®12% per cent
higher than Â»t the close of the previous sales,
the feelins In the local market Is very firm
and the trend of values is unmistakably up
ward. There is nn especially good demand for
fin.- staple WfÂ«.ls. MaÂ»y of the trade believe
that this kind of stock is bended toward 65c,
and there are not wanting those who are pre
dicting even 70c for this .-lass of stock.
The sales of the week In Boston amount to
0,117.000 pounds domestic and 395,000 pounds
foreign, making a total of 9.512,000, against a
total of g.oatf.OOO for the previous week and a
totaJ of i.584.000 for the corresponding week
last year. Sales since January 1. 1599, amount
to 209,R46,000 pounds, against 83,764,010 pounds
last year at this time.
London Wool Sales.
LONDON. Sept. 20.â offerings at the
wool auction sales to-day numbered 15,067 bales.
including a good selection of Queensland new
crop. The latter was in better condition than
the IS9B crop and the home trade and the Con
tinent were eager bidders for this grade.
Scoured merinos were well represented and sold
principally to Yorkshire at hardening rates.
The Continent operated reservedly and Is evi
dently waiting for the opening of the Aus
tralian sales next week. Swan River sold read-
II: to the home trade. Lambs were in excep
tionally strong demand and merinos sold at 15
per '?:it advance. Crossbreds were in sternly
request at prices ranging from 10 to 15 per sent
higher, Yorkshire taking the bulk. Fine
creasy croasbreds were quickly disposed of.
Medium low erossbreds. were largely repre
sented and brought out keen competition, with
the home trade the best buyer at decidedly
harder rates. A good selection of washed
cross stripes was taken by the home trade at
an advance of 10 per cent.

THE STOCK MARKET.
<?â â â â <s>
There was a (food business in securities on
the morning session, but fluctuations were few
and narrow. Hutchinson Plantation sold down
to $29 50 and Gas it Electric to $66.
In the afternoon Makawell Plantation sold
down to $4S 25 and Pauuhau 10 $37.
Mining stocks stood about the same as on
the preceding day. The telegram from the
pump said: "At C:2O this morning the water
was --2 feet 4 Inches below the 19G0 level station.
No. 2 elevator has been running continuously."
The Overman assessment falls delinquent In
board to-day.
Bullion is assessed 5 cents.
The Santa Rosalia Mining Company has de
clared a dividend of 5 cents, payable on the
25th.
The Comstock Pumping Association had $7848
cash in its treasury on September 15.
The Champion Mining Company of Nevada
County has declared its forty-ninth dividend.
The rate is 25 cents per share.
The Isabella Mining Company of Colorado has
declared a dividend of 1 cent per share,
j amounting to $22,500, payable Immediately. This
is the first dividend since June. 1899, and will
make $202,500 for the year and $472,500 from the
start.
Dividends were paid yesterday as follows:
New York and Honduras Rosarlo in Central
America, $15,000; Raven mine in British Co
â  lumbia, $10,000.
The dividend of $2,000,000 by the Calumet and
Hecla Copper mine of Michigan will be paid
on the 29th.

I Funston Avenges His Wounded Horse
Colonel Funston sat on his horse,
watching his . Kansas boys lire at the
enemy at Caloocan, when an orderly came
up with the commanding general's com
pliments and an order to stop firing.
"Cease tiring!" shouted the. colonel.
Only a few of the nearest, men heard
him and the firing kept on.
"Cease firing" ordered the colonel
again, and this time the bugle gave forth :
the peal and the firing, after scattering , â 
stopped altogether. Just then a ball from ;
the insurgents drilled the neck of the
colonel's pony. Quick as a flash Funston
whirled around, fire in his eyes.
"Commence firing!" he shouted at the
top of his voice. Give 'em at nine
hundred yards!" '
It took three or four rounds of ammuni
tion all along the line to avenge that
horse, though the colonel still rides it.â
Manila Correspondent In Leslie's Weekly..

MILK CONTRACT
CANNOT BUY A
VOTE FOR BURNS
Bliss Will Never Go
Into the Corral.
Oakland Olrice San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Sept. 20.
Somewhere In Europe In the breast of
Senator Fred Stratton is securely locked
the key to the political situation in Ala
meda County. It is the general opinion
that Senator Stratton does not wish to
succeed himself. He has more than in
timated that such is his intention, butâ
When the Senator left Oakland a few
months ago his primary law had not
been tested, and consequently when he
talked of rearing from public life he
was not aware of the boom that its suc
cess has since given to its author. The
knowledge of whether he will or will
not retire would be a source of great sat
isfaction at this time, when lines are
already being laid for the general cam
paign next year.
Assemblyman Bliss is already a candi
date for Senatorial honors. The same
supporters who put him in the Assembly
declare their ability to place him in the
Senate. Mr. Bliss is ambitious, but not
aggressive. Personally he would prefer
to succeed himself as Assemblyman, but
circumstances may force him into the
Senatorial race.
Just now Mr. Bliss is a very interesting
political factor. For several weeks it
has been generously whispered that the
Assemblyman had bet bought by the
followers of Dan Burns; and that the
particular bait is the contract Mr. Bliss
now holds for plying all the dairy
products to the Pullman Company. Add
ed to this, a local paper a few days ago
published something complimentary to
Bliss, and concluded with an apologetic
statement that perhaps the !a*t Senato
rial fight at Sacramento had been fought
on very narrow lines, and that now the
legislators have had time to think they
might be inclined toward more liberality.
This was construed as inferring that Mr.
Bliss, who was a pronounced Grant man
and a pronounced anti-Burns man, might
have changed his mind.
Mr. Bliss has not changed his mind and
will never vote for Colonel Burns for
United States Senator.
"That story of ing bought by the
privilege of supplying butter and cream
to the Pullman Company," said Mr.
Bliss, "is one of the funniest I have
heard for a long time. It is true I have
that contract, and it is just as true that
I have had it all the years I have been
in business and an age before I war ever
thought of in politics. It is a wonder that
it is not reported that the contract has
been taken.Â«*away from me, for my re
fusal to vote for Colonel Burns. But I
do not anticipate any such action. When
I was first elected the newspapers in siz
ing up the Senatorial situation placed
me either with the Burn* men or in the
doubtful list, and one of them published
a story that I was to be reached through
my brother-in-law who was represented
to be a well paid employe of the Southern
Pacific. It happens that I have no
brother-in-law, nor any other relative,
however remote, employed by the rail
road company. In Oakland a man can
hear anything by talking to himself and
1 have learned that this is the met! by
which many so-called 'political rumors'
emanating from "a source of undoubted
authority' have their birth.
"Before Mr. Grant went north I visited
him in San Diego. We discussed the Sen
atorial situation. I asked him If in the
event of an extra session he would again
be a candidate. He said that he would
n<jver be a candidate again, if it meant
getting into another fight such as was
waged at Sacramento this spring. I
understood from this conversation that it
in unlikely he will again seek Senatorial
"Strange as it may seem, with all the
reported political workings that have been
going on in regard to an extra session and
the calling of a caucus, I have never been
asked to Elgn a call for a caucus since the
Legislature adjourned, and neither have I
been asked to vote for any particular
candidate. My constituents do not want
Colonel Burns, and that ends it. 1 am
ulways ready to go into a caucus if by
so doing the Republicans can agree upon
a representative man, but I should never
be bound by any caucus as regards my
vote on United States Senator. I cer
tainly would not agree to enter a caucus
liA^d on the principle of dropping the
weakest candidate each time and binding
everybody to vote for the- man having the
most votes at the finish.
â â¢I have talked with Senator Stratton on
the question of the State Senatorship and
he gave me very plainly to understand
that he would not be a candidate to suc
ceed himself. This Impression seems to
be very general, and I find that he gave
Jt to many of his friends. If I were asked
what I would like to do I would prefer to
succeed myself as Assemblyman, but
these matters have to be adjusted, like
everything else, and if I find that it is
expedient to attempt to succeed Mr. Strat
ton I shall certainly make the attempt.
I do not feel that anything I did at Sac
ramento this spring needs an apology,
and I may say without egotism that since
my turn I have been thoroughly con
vinced that my course was in harmony
with the wishes of the people who Bent
rr.e there."
Assemblyman Joe Kelly, when asked re
cently if he had been requested to sign a
rail "for a Republican caucus, made the
very honest reply: "No. I have never
cu-en such a paper. 7 understand, though,
that one is being circulated, but I guess
ihey know where I stand, anyhow, and
they don't feel it necessary to run after
me." Mr. Kelly, however, omitted to
explain who was represented by "they."
Howard "Wright moved his residence
from East Oakland as soon after the ad-
Ir.urnment of the Legislature as was con
venient, and it Is understood he Is now
living somewhere on BernaJ Heights. He
has not figured in politics since last
March, and hi district Is already can
vassing its available material for his suc
cessor.

cry Fix months, when I make out

"It was hf-re that the error occurred,
for in making the copy I failed to check
off all the discharges.

THE SAN FRANCISCO (ALL, THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 21, 1899.
SAYS NEGROES ARE
PREPARING FOR RACE WAR
OAKLAND. Sept. 20.â Rev. J. L, Allen,
pastor of the Beth Eden Baptist Church,
which is an organization of colored peo
ple, has expressed some radical ideas re
! garding the increasing race troubles in
I the Southern States. For many years he
lived in the South. He says:
This last evilâ that of lynching â must be fol
lowed by anotherâ a race war, which, in my
mind, seems Inevitable.
Of course the problems of the South between
the whites and blacks could be settled without
war: but this involves the taking of an entirely
different standâ one toward the other â by both
the whites and blacks. It must be admitted
that a great many negroes are Ignorant, but I
believe that those who do understand the real
' situation have been responsible for misleading
the others. The negroes should let politics j
: the whites should let the negroes alone
and permit them to vote as they please. I say
there is no real need of it. if some precautions
were taken, but 1 am tearful that a war must
be Neither the white nor ths black understimd
each other. They will fight as long as they
i rannnt uniWptand each other.
I look for this war any time; should not be
i surprise! to find it coming on within the next
few weeks In the nature of uprisings. In the
I long run there is only one way for such a race
', war to end, but the public, in my belief, under-
I estimates the strength of the negroes. Very
j many of the blacks in the South are men of
lerable means, and these, so I am md
, have b<^n turning their property hold-
Ingt Int c in and laying up stores of arms and
] ammunition by way of preparation for a rare
I conflict. I am Inclined to think that God will
i be on the r.^trro Fide, and that the result of
i such a conflict would be for good. It is clear
! to my mind that the races nrver can be sepa
! rated. They cannot live one without the other.
â  No two races can live separately in this coun
try under its system of government.
How the war will change the social status of
the negro I do not care to predict.

Died Away From Home.
William Flannigan, a soldier, died in the
Receiving Hospital last evening presum
ably from Brlght's disease. Flannig-an
had bt-en drinking heavily and was taken
to the hospital yesterday morning and
treated for alcoholism. He was dis
charged shortly after noon, but proceeded
to celebrate the event by inbibing more
liquor. Last evening he was found at
Fifth and Market streets unconscious and
removad to the Receiving Hospital, where
lie died a short time afterward.
SHOCK OF THE
TRIAL MAY RILL
JOHN NICHOLL
So Declares Expert
Dr. Woolsey.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, Sept. 20.
The features to-day at the trial of the
$50,000 breach of promise suit of Jane
Hodge against the aged capitalist John
Nicholl, who is familiarly known as the
"Duke of Richmond," was the expert
testimony given by Dr. E. S. Woolsey. J.
S. Eastman and Frank L. Adams; some
warm cross-firing between the attorneys
and the much longed for story of Mazie
Nicholl, whom the aged plaintiff regards
as the prime cause and instigator of the
breach of promise.
When the trial was resumed this fore
noon the courtroom was crowded with
spectators and Attorney Foote's cross-ex
amination of the aged defendant, Mr.
Nicholl, was continued. He declared that
he had never told his daughters of his
alleged engagement to Miss Hodge, but
that the latter had broken this news to
them. Regarding the spicy letter received
by Miss Hodge over his signature defend
ant frankly admitted he had dictated it*
contents.
Attorney Foote was anxious to learn
what church Nlcholl attended and the
First Presbyterian proved to be the one.
Mr. Foote openly admitted he was not
up on churches and Mr. Nicholl face
tiously remarked that "it is time you
were."
"You are known Â«hy the name of 'Duke
of Richmond' sometimes?" queried At
torney Foote.
"Yes, that and many other names," re"
sponded the witness, who at one time
owned Point Richmond, now belonging to
the Valley Railroad, "and BOmetfmes my
daughters call me 'Duke' at home, just
joking." Thereafter matters pertaining
to Mr. Nicholl'B health during last No
vember were gone into and it appeared
that during that month witness had not
been well.
Dr. E. S. Woolsey was called this after
noon to give expert testimony as to the
aged capitalist's condition. He graphic
ally described his method of examination
of the defendant, related the fact that
Nicholl had nearly fainted at the time
and that in his opinion, briefly. Nicholl
was enfeebled. The doctor declared that
any shock would be likely to kill the de
fendant. "In fact." he continued, "it is
my opinion that the shock of this trial
will kill him." To this statement Mr.
Foote vigorously objected. Dr. Woolsey
also declared that matrimony in Nicholl 3
case might prove immediately fatal.
When Dr. Woolsey had been disposed of
he took a seat beside defendant's counsel
and began coaching Attorneys McKee and
Mhoon in their examination of Dr. J. S.
Eastman who was next called. Attorney
Foote objected to this, declared it was un
fair and referred to Dr. Woolsey as "the
main guy" of the defense's experts.
Judge Ogden, however, ruled that the
doctor's action would not beobjectionablo.
Dr. Eastman, as well as Dr. Frank L.
Vdams who followed on the stand, both
testified that they had examined de
fendant's physical condition; but while
each summed up his various ailments
about the same, neither would vouch as
to any absolute prool of feebleness.
The" star witness of the defense. Miss
Mazie Nicholl, was now called, and all
eyes were turned upon her. She. was
gowned In black, with white front and
pretty blue collar. Around her neck also
loosely hung a black boa. Her hat was
forgeously decorated with green feathers.
ler hair, Sprinkled with gray, gave her
a matronly appearance. Yet she spoke
quite dramatically, her big black eyes
snapping at times when objections and
interruptions were made while she gave
her Btory, which seemed exceedingly well
memorized. Once while her father's
counsel made a slip by referring to her
as "Now. Miss Hodge," Mazie s color
rose to her cheeks.

"FLEEING FROM POMPEII."
Marble Group Presented to Art Asso
ciation by George Crocker.
Mr. and Mrs. George Crocker have pre-
Bented to the San Francisco Art Associa
tion a mapniflcent marble group entitled
"Fleeing from Pompeii." The subject
treated is an incident of the overwhelm
ing of Pompeii by the eruption of Vesu
vius. It represents the flight of a man
and woman through the showers of ashes,
smoke and lava that destroyed the ancient
city. The woman carries an infant in one
arm. and all three figures are gracefully
modeled.
The group is about eight feet high and
rests on a circular pedestal three feet in
height on which is carved several figures
typical of the life of that period. The
statuary is in white marble and is the
work of the famous sculptor Bonzoni, who
flourished some twenty years ago. The
original cost was $20,000. It is now in Mr.
Crocker's parlor, but will be transferred
to the art association in a day or two.
Drs. Tillman and Roche Return.
Doctors F. J. Tlllman and T. B. Roche,
two young County Hospital internes, who
became externes on the Ist of last July
and who have distinguished tbemselvi s
by kindness and sympathy to the poor in
the hospital as well as among their
brothers of the profession, have returned
from an extensive deer hunting expedi
tion through the northern counties. The
young medicos left here at the beginning
of April and in company with G. T. Smith
of Berkeley and Harry Tillman. hired
a two-horse team and started off from
San Rafael. During their absence they
had the rare good luck of turning over
one dozen bucks. One of these was
shipped to their companion. Dr. T. J.
Crowley. assistant at the County
Hospital
TO LIFT THE DEBT OF
NEW YOUTHS' DIRECTORY
GENERAL COMMITTEE EFFECTS
PERMANENT ORGANIZATION.
Will Endeavor to Raise Any Deficit
Which May Remain After
Applying Proceeds of the
Grand Bazaar.
The general committee having In charge
the approaching fair and bazaar in aid
of the new Youths' Directory at the sug
gestion of Chairman Kelly decided at a
meeting last night to perpetuate its or
ganization until the last cent of the $35,
--000 debt which confronts the institution
is paid. While it is confidently expected
that a large sum will be realized by the
fair, which will commence on September
2S at Armory Hall, Market street, near
Tenth, still it is presumed that there will
remain a deficit, and to overcome this the
committee will continue its labors.
Chairman Costello of the. committee on
programme reported that interesting fea
tures will be presented every evening dur
ing the fair, and tiie ladles of the dif
ferent booths will have "nights at home,"
at which there will be vocal and instru
mental selections.
The ladies drew lots for choice of posi
tions for the booths with the following
result: No. 1, Saint Rose's; 2, candy
booth; 3, St. Dominick's; 4. charity; 5,
Mission Dolores; 6, St. Anthony's; 7, SL
Joseph's Union; 8, St. Patrick's; 9, St.
Charles; 10, League of the Cross; 11, hos
pital; 12, St. Mary's; 13, Sacred Heart;
14, St. Anne's.
The executive committee has the dcc
i oration in hand and will be assisted by
j the ladies in charge. They will all meet
: at the Armory next Monday morning to
arrange details.
The chairman appointed George Gal
lagher, W. A. Derham and H. J. Fisher

will also act as a press committee. J. B.
! Mclntyre, G. B. Gallagher, 11. J. Maher

I various organizations of the city for spe-
I cial nights.
David J. Costello was appointed poet

i will compose a poem to be read at the
! opening of the fair.
Two scholarships for a boy and a girl
I have been offered by two of the leading
business colleges and will be contested
for by popular vote. J. H. Rossiter has

erous givers.
There will bo two musieales given in

SOL CAHEN RETURNS.
Past Department Commander of the
G. A. R. Will Tell Garfield
Post of His Visit East.
Past Department Commander Sol Cahen
of the Grand Army of the Republic re
turned yesterday from Philadelphia,
where he was one of the representatives
from the department of California and
Nevada at the National Encampment of
the G. A. R. On his way home he had
as companion Pension Commissioner H.
Clay Evans, who is on his way to this
city. Mr. Evans stopped at Salt Lake for
a "short stay. During his absence of a
month Mr. Cahen remained a week in
Philadelphia in attendance on the en
campment, and then went to Washing
ton, D. C. where he paid a visit to Presi
dent McKinlry. Next Tuesday night he
will attend a meeting of Garfield Post
and will tell of his visit in Washington
and to places that were made historic
by the War of the Rebellion.

Richmond in for Improvements.
Progressive Richmond, through its im- \
provement club, will demand its share of !
the tax levy. In several sections there are
choked sewers and drifting sands which
will be catalogued among the nuisances
of the district. At a meeting of the Point
Loboa Improvement Club on Tuesday
President George B. Fletcher stated for
the Information of the executive commit
tee that the time has arrived when certain
Improvements had become a necessity.
Among subjects of complaint was the bad
condition of a blind sewer on Thirty-first
avenue, which had been permitted to re
tain the offal of a cow ranch in the neigh
borhood to the injury of owners of prop
erty, who would otherwise build on their
lots and add to the wealth of the district.
Drifting sands which were permitted by
the owners of vacant lots had become a
nuisance which was Intolerable and an
in justice to those property owners who
had improved their streets and sidewalks.
John J. Cassldy, secretary of the execu
tive committee, with J. M. Wilkins, chair
man of the same body, drew the atten
tion of the club to the want of more lights
through the entire valley. On the recom- ;
mendatlon of Mr. Wilkins the secretary
was instructed to communicate with the
Supervisors and request that body to pave
the city's portion of Fulton street, from
First avenue to Stanyan street, alongside
of Golden Gate Park, the property own
ers haying paved their portion on the
north side.
On motion of Secretary W. P. Johnson
thj 5 chair appointed the following commit
tee on the monthly social which will be>
held in the club rooms on the night of Oc
tober 28: J. T. Williams (chairman), pres
ident; George R. Fletcher, v. W. Wench
ell, Ed GJnley, A. Hlmenover, W. P. John
son and P. M. McGushin. The admission
will be by tickets at 25 cents, and a dance
will follow the literary exercises.
Eureka Society in Hot Water.
There is another rumpus in the Eureka
Society for the Protection of Children.
Ex-Secretary Wadham resigned as a di
rector yesterday and he was followed by
D. S. O'Brien nixl J. C. Irvine. They,:
with W. H. Treighler. propose to form
another society to he known as the San :
Francisco Society for the Protection of
Children. The statement has been made
that the trouble was caused by the fact
that "VVadham ran the Eureka society as
a sort of divorce bureau in the interests
of his legal friend O'Brien. For every
charge there Is a counter-charge. The :
directors of the Eureka body at once filled |
the vacancies by electing- L. A. Albora.
O. M. Ratto and S. T. Dodson. It still
holds the Eureka Boys' Home, where
there are about three dozen children.
There is some apprehension that Wad
ham will make trouble with regard to
the custody of some of these children
over whom he was appointed guardian
during the time he was secretary of the
Eureka society. He says li^ will retain
the guardianship over a number.
Driven From Home.
The officers of the Society for the Pre
vention of Cruelty to Children are
i puzzling over the case of a little waif
which fell into their hands yesterday.
The daughters of Mrs. Mary Garbarino
of 1117 Broadway found a little girl
wandering on Pacific street, near Mason,
whose face was badly cut. She said that
the wounds were Inflicted by her father,
who struck her when she asked him fur
bread. The child gave her name as Anna
ii( Qrody, and said that she was 8 years
I of age, and that her folks lived in Salmon
alley, off Pacific street. Mrs. Garbarino
kindly cared for the waif until yesterday,
when' Officer Shannon inrule an unsatls
i factory investigation. He failed to find
! any one of the name given in Salmon
alley, and no one there knew the child.
! She" was placed with the Ladies' Pro
! tection and Relief Society.
Chinese Gamblers Arrested.
The San Francisco Chinese Merchant.**'
Club at 127 Waverly place was raided last
evening by Sergeant Duke and posse and
thirty-seven prisoners taken. The entire
fan-tan outlit was captured and the raid
was one of the most successful for some
time. The capture was effected by Offi
cer John Conlan, who masqueraded as a
Chinaman and gained an entrance to the
gambling room. His mnke-up was com
plete and the wily heathens were taken
by surprise. The prisoners were locked
up at the California street police sta
tion.
