Casey Breaks an Ankle.
Jackie Casey celebrated his release
from custody by imbibing too much In
toxicating liquor Tuesday night, ami early
yesterday morning- he was arrested on
Polk street by Officer Charleston and
booked for "drunk." Yesterday morn
ing, when ho recovered his senses, he
found that he could not use his left
tinkle. He was taken to the Receiving
Hospital by Detectives J. B. Gibson and
Ellis, where Dr. Heintz found that the
ankle bone was fractured. He was later
removed to the Waldeek Sanitarium.
CROSSED WIRES START A FIRE.
An Early Morning Blaze on Market
Street, Near Powell.
A fire which caused much damage to
the basements of the business-houses on
the first part of the ten-hundred block
of Market street occurred about 2 o'clock
this morning. It is supposed to have been
i aused by the short circuiting of wires
under the tailor-shop of A. Jacobs at 1010
Market street. Its discovery was due
to the sudden extinguishing "of the elec
tric lights in the saloon of Kelly & Bren
nan. at 1016 Market, which caused one
of the proprietors, Harry Brennan, to
make an investigation, resulting in the
location of the blaze.
An alarm was rung in from box 47, and
when tlie firo department arrived clouds
of smoke were Issuing from many of the
stores, Much damage was caused by
smoke and water, and the department
worked nearly one hour before the lire
was entirely < xtinguished.
Yerba Buena Lodge.
The members of Yerba Buena Lodge of
the Knights of Honor entertained their
friends at a smoker in the social hall of
the Alcazar building on Monday, and in
addition to cigars, pipes and tobacco there
was served up a good supper. Among
the many who were present were Grand
Dictator Archibald, Grand Assistant Dic
tator Salomon. Past Grand Assistant Dic
tator .Learned, Grand Reporter John
stone, Grand Trustee Joy and District
Deputy Summers. After the supper had
been partaken of A. M. McPherson, P. J.
Denny, W. H. Howard, Arthur Logan,
J. E. Erickson, C. P. Wedekind, F. F.
Wedekind, R. H. Noble and J. W. Ed
monds, the committee on social hour, took
charge and entertained those present with
a good programme of sours, instrumental
music, gramaphone selections and an ad
dress on the work of the order by Thomas
Johnstone, the grand reporter, it was
one of the most pleasant smokers that
this lodge has ever tdven.
A Supposed Incendiary Fire.
\n alarm was turned in yesterday after
noon at 4 o'clock for a fire on Cotter |
street, opposite the Jewish Orphanage j
Asylum on the Mission road. The damage j
amounted tv about $23. The fire was sup- !
posed to be the work of an incendiary.
The property damaged belonged to a man
named Anderson, who was absent at the
time, and shortly before the tire was dis
covered two ladies, Mrs. Hallock, resid
ing on Francis street, and Mrs. Kock,
residing at 23 Cotter street, two doors
from where the fire started, saw a man
cuming out from the rear of the building
at 19 Cotter street, where the fire oc
curred. Immediately after he left the
premises smoke was seen to emerge from
the back portion of the building.
The ladies were unable to give an accu
rate description of the man further than
to say that he was a tall, thin fellow,
with a high white collar, and dressed in
a dark suit of clothes. Detectivps Fitz
gerald and Graham are on the case.
Policeman Freel Fined.
Policeman John Freel, whose ambition
is to be a detective, was fined $2f> by the
Police Commissioners last night for being
drunk. Several days ago Fred reported
for duty in an intoxicated condition and,
swelled with his own importance and In
cidentally his "jap." he starti-d to dic
tate to Captain AYittman as to how to run
the department. Wittman sent him home
and the following day he preferred
charges against him. After listening to
the evidence In the case the Commission
ers charitably imposed a fine instead of
dismissing the offender from the depart
ment.

FARC E COMEDY
PRESENTED AT
THE ALCAZAR,
ii Â» WAKE and personally present!"
A might be a good answer to roll
Â£\ call at the Alcazar this week, for
"Too Much Johnson" seems to be
drawing a good half the theater going
public, and to sleep were an impossi
bilityânoise is rampant. In spite of the
title. Billings Is the manâ and what a
man. The fact that one can lie is a fatal
defect and skill argues a long apprentice
ship. If the fellow ever gave himself an
i introspective look, the shock would blind
! himâ he is the very prince of liars, and
because of this, adored of the audience.
Not being required morally to agitate
I the public over farce-comedy, let him
settle the case with his own creditors.
The audience, considering him in the
person of Eugene Ormonde, are his
debtors. He is a quietly triumphant
rascal, never too slow but once, when
he holds the first curtain tableau a shade
too long, and beautifully wooden and
unemotional to the end. Miss Howe, as
the mother-in-law, is the first of the
w.,rnen not overdoing tho bustling splrli
I and seldom too loud. The two yoiing
things, Mr. Denithorne and Miss Henry,
out to capture the conditions of happi
ness, are merely background, and as
such, unimportant.
Miss Foster does her almost nothing
quietly and well. Mr. Webster is a
ferocious Mr. Johnson, but very funny.
He would be equally funny if his voice
were tamed or even crippled. There is
I not a serious moment in the action, and
I constant laughter is inevitable. Next
i week "The Idler" is the bill.

The Columbia opens its doors on Mon
day evening with Madame Modjeska in
"Marie Antoinette." Clinton Stuart, the
author, claims original treatment of an
old theme.
The Orpheum offers an exceptional

Ing room only is the result. Nexi
Jennie Yeamans, Frank Cushman, the
minstrel, and Seymour and Dupree are the

sung.

fitted, will open Sunday, October 1. with

man."

with "Lohengrin" and "Othello." The
I popularity of the latter warrants its be-
I ing run another week. It will alternate
I with "Martha."

Braunhart Perjury Case.
When the perjury proceedings pending
against Sammy Braunhart were taken up
yesterday morning before Judge Graham
"the defendant's attorney. Garret Mc
l Enerney, stated that he had only received
i a transcript of the evidence from the
rourt stenographer the night before am',
for that reason he requested Judge Gra
ham to continue the case. Judge Graham
continued Braunhart's case until 9 o'clock
! this morning, when the arguments will
I be heard.
Smuggled Silks on the Sherman.
Customs searchers found yesterday on
the Government transport Sherman a
i quantity of silk dress goods hidden near
i the powder magazine. The bundles bore
the names of United States officers who
; returned from Manila on the transport.
! The goods were seized and confiscated by
j the Federal authorities, but will be de
i live red to the owners on payment of the
duty owing on the articles, there being
a concession made in the case of sol
diers.

JOSEPH D. GRANT MENTIONED FOR THE MAYORALTY.
RELATIVES TO WELCOME
BOYS OF THE FOURTEENTH
CALIFORNIANS WILL BE GIVEN
A FITTING RECEPTION.
Citizens' Committee Will Help and
Obstacles Will Be O-vercome.
Tribute to North. Dakotas.
An enthusiastic meeting of the rela
tives and friends of the Calif or nians in
the fourteenth Regular Infantry, who
are expected to arrive home on the City
of Para early next week, was hel<l in the
Ellis-street armory last night for the
purpose of receiving the report of the
committee appointed to confer with the
citizens' committee in the matter of pro
viding a fitting welcome to the brave
Eoldier boys. The fact that the report of
this committee was somewhat in the
nature of a disappointment did not for a
moment dampen the enthusiasm, but
served rather to strengthen the deter
mination of the relatives' association to
carry out their cherished plans for a
grand reception.
President John McGeary called the
meeting to order, and after the miutes
of the previous meeting had been read by
the secretary, Miss Philita Mohun,
Chairman J. H. Burson road the report
of the committee, the substance of which
was that while the citizens' committee
was willing and anxious to do every
thing in its power to give the boys a wel
come, there were serious obstacles which
must tirst be overcome. The first ob
stacle was contained in a letter from
General Shafter stating that the Four
teenth would lie landed at Angel Island,
where they would be paid off and brought
to this city 200 or 800 at a time.
The second was the announced declara
tion of the Harbor Commission that the
nave of the ferry building would not he
available for future banquets. The citi
zens' committee, however, promised that
every effort would be made to Induce
General Shafter to permit the landing of
the soldiers in San Francisco in a body,
to Induce the Harbor Commission to
reconsider its decision, and further, to
furnish a tug and a band to meet the
transport as she enterd the harbor.
It was decided to engage a steamer on
which tho relatives could also go out to
mÂ»Â»e.t the City of Para, and I^s tickets
were 6poken for on the spot.
During the evening the association gave
a warm testimonial of lta appreciation
of the friendship existing between the
North Dakota volun teens, several hun
9red of whom wore present by invitation,
Rnd the California boys in the Fourteenth
Infantry, by adopting the following reso
lution.-?, which were read by J. R. Baird:
Whereas, We, the friends and rela
tives of the volunteers serving in the
Fourteenth Regular Infantry, have as
sembled In duly organized meeting,
and
Whereas, We recognise the many
acts of kindness and of friendship
shown by tho officers and men of the
North Dakota volunteers to the vol
unteers of the Fourteenth Infantry
djjring the Philippine campaign, and,
Whereas, We feel that it is our duty,
in the absence of our boys, to thank
the officers and men of the North Da
kota volunteers; therefore, be it
Resolved, That the North Dakota
volunteers have our heartfelt thanks
and our best wishes that thoy may at
tain the reward of true friends and
brave patriots; and be it further
Resolved, That these resolutions be
spread upon the minutes of this as
sociation and that copies be forwarded
to the North Dakota volunteers and to
the press.
Mr. Baird had hardly resumed his seat
n-hen the North Dakotans rose in a body
tnd cheered the relatives' association
antil the building shook. The latter re
ipomled with a will and then a committee
lonsistlnK of Mrs. Kennedy, Mrs. Walker
Hid Mrs Baird was appointed to go out
10 the Presidio to-day and present copies
If the resolutions to the North Dakota
â egiment. The association then adjourned
|o meet Saturday night In Balboa Hall, In
Iho Native 6ons" building.

Grant's name at tho head of the Re
publican municipal ticket would inspire
the masses of Republican voters with
confidence of victory
Mr Grant now occupies the office of
president of the San Francisco Art Asso
ciation a position once tilled by James D.
Phelan. lie has succeeded Phelan so ad
mirably at the head of the association,
that the line of succession may be ex
tended to the head of the municipal
government. .._-..* â¢
Mr Grant is a merchant of character.
â He has traveled extensively throughout
the civilized world, and paid especial at
tention to modern methods in the govern
ment of large and progressive cities.
â¢ Mr. Grant has not yet signified a
willingness to surrender the time which
his business affairs demand in order to
accept public service.
The Democratic convention will meet
again next Tuesday evening. The con
test among aspirants for the office of
District Attorney is becoming quite ani
mated. The Iroquois brave* have re
solved that every man who failed to sup
port Bryan for President in 1896 shall be
excluded from the list of the acceptable.
This resolution is regarded as a declara
tion against Charles Wesley Reed.
R. Porter Ashe is gaining strength
among the delegates, and his nomina
tion for District Attorney would be no
surprise. Many delegates believe that he
would win out by reason of his personal
popularity when another candidate might
lose.
The Democratic Municipal Convention,
committee on platform and resolutions
will meet to-night at 8 o'clock at the
headquarters of the Democratic County
Committee.
The Forty-first delegation to the Re
publican convention held a meeting yes
terday at the residence of Sheldon G.
Kellogg, 192S Vallejo street, and indorsed
the following candidates: W. G. Sy'el,
son-in-law or President Kelly of the
liihernia Bank, for Assessor; W. G.
Doane, for Auditor: John Farnham, for
Public Administrator; John l,ackmann,
for Sheriff; G. P. Sanderson, son of ex-
Mayor Sanderson, for Supervisor.

PART BASALT BLOCKS,
THE REST BITUMINIZED
THE PECULIAR CONDITIONS ON
MONTGOMERY STREET.
T. J. Clunie Declines to Act With
Other Property-Owners and the
Result Is a Disfigured.
Pavement.
Considerable curiosity was aroused yes- !
terday afternoon regarding the paving, of ;
the block on Montgomery street, between
Pine and California. It was noticed that
the block was being bltuminized, the con
crete foundation having already been laid,
with the exception of fifty-nine feet in
front of the property belonging to T. J.
| Clunie. On this portion the workmen
! were busy putting .<ack the old basalt
I blocks which formerly constituted the
; pavement.
The explanation of the matter lies in
the differences tuat exist between Clunie
and the City Street â¢ Improvement Com
pany, which was recently authorized by
resolution of the Board of Supervisors to
proceed with the work. The city is to
stand $490 of the expense, and the prop
j erty owners the balance. According to J. !
W. McDonald, the president of the pay-
I ing company, ail the property owners
| agreed to have the work done under the
| conditions placed by the Board of Super
: visors.
"1 had the street torn up," said Mc-
Donald, "ready to be paved, and my rep
resentative went to see Clunie's manager,
j J. H. Todd, in regard to the proposition.
He was told what the cost would be, and
I he, replied that he could have the work
: done for $45, though Todd did not offer 113
I that amount. My representative informed
.him that it was impossible to do it for
$45, as that would bo at the rate of 6
> conts a foot, which would not pay for the
j concrete, i As ho refused to treat with us
! there was only one thing to do, and that
was to put the basalt blocks back in
place."
Manager Todd when seen in regard to
j the affair said: "We have no desire to
' stand in the way of public Improvements
whatever. McDonald Is working under
an old agreement signed by all the prop
i erty owners except the Stevenson estate,
from whom General Clunle bought this
! property. We have never signed the pe
j tition, and do not feel bound by it. Me
' Donald's man did not come to see us
! until the street was torn up. I had al
ready practically signed a contract with
the Pacific Paving Company for the same ;
; kind of pavement now in front of the
Phelan Duilding, consisting of wooden
j blocks covered with bitumen, which I
consider superior to the other pavement.
When McDonald set his figure it was so
much higher than that of my choice, of
pavement that I refused to entertain it.
In a moment of pique, I suppose, he had
! the blocks put back. We will shortly
put down the pavement which we were
â¢originally In favor of. and that will end i
the matter,"

GEORGE CROCKER
WILL NOT ADMIT
THE LARGE DEAL
The Speyer Buy Is
Still a Mystery.
CONFIRMED BY INDICATIONS
EVERYTHING POINTS TO A SUB
STANTIATION.
H. E. Hunting-ton Will in All Likeli
hood Be the Supreme Power
in the Southern Pacific
Company Here.
Not-withstanding the secrecy maintained
by the Crockers at this end of the South
i crn Pacific Company's line and the close
] neBB with which Speyer keeps his lips
j shut at the other end, it is generally ad
mitted In the big yellow building on Mont
gomery street thut the Crocker interest
will soon exist only as a memory of the
past and that Huntingdon will In truta
be the Southern Pacific.
The report that the transfer of stock
has been nia.de and that the. price paid
by Speyer was $13,<X>U,i)00 is considered
very premature. It is believed thai the

AN ATTEMPTED SUICIDE
FOLLOWS LOVE QUARREL
|-|- IZZIE MURPHY, a good looking servant girl, tried to end her life with
; carbolic acid yesterday. She had a quarrel with Frank Onettl, her
' I lover, and when he refused to make It up she placed a two-ounce vial
' -Iâ i of the deadly drug to her lips and would have swallowed It all had not
' Onettl knocked the bottle out of her hand.
> Lizzie lives with her cousin, Martin Maker, who keeps the lodging house
, at 1017 Broadway. Onettl works in the Gibraltar warehouse. The couple
5 have been lovers for nearly two years and were to have been married next
' month. Miss Murphy, however, took a fancy to another young man and
? went out with him a couple of times, much to the disgust of Onettl. After a
' heated argument one day he persuaded Lizzie to go to his brother Joe's place
5 on Sutter Creek. Joe is a married man and is doing well at placer mining.
p She remained there for some time and only came back to 1017 Broadway last
; Saturday.
5 The couple met last Tuesday nlpht and quarreled. Lizzie brooded over
â¢ it and early yosterday morning purchased the carbolic acid. At noon she
! met Frank as he was returning to hia home from the warehouse, for his
. lunch, and asked him to lot bygones bo bygones. Onetti said he did not
want to sco anything more of her. It was then she swallowed the carbolic
â¢ acid.
Onetti picked her up from the sidewalk where she was writhing in
! agony and carried her to 1017 Broadway, where Bhe was given an emetic.
, The Harbor Hospital was then rung up and In a few minutes sho was in the
operating room in the institution. Drs. Morrison and Folkers worked over
I her for half an hour and then pronounced her out of danger. Miss Murphy
le badly burned with the acid about the mouth and face and will be able to
' swallow nothing but liquids for some time to come.

GIGANTIC RAILWAY TRUST.
Significance of Rockefeller's
Entry Into New York. Cen
tral Directorate.
NEW YORK, Sept. 20.â The Journal
says: "A billion dollar railroad combine
is foreshadowed by the entry of "William
Rockefeller into the directory of the New
York Central.
"Tho union of Rockefeller and Vandcr
bilt transportation interests throughout
the country would result in the manage
ment of The principal railway systems of
tho country passing under the control of
two families. A gigantic railroad trust
would thus be formed which would eclipse
In magnitude anything in the line of com
binations this country has yet seen.
"The combined wealth of Rockefeller
Brothersâ John D., Wiliam and Frank
on a conservative estimate reaches five
hundred million dollars, and William K.
VanderWlt the heirs of Cornelius and the
other branches of the Vanderbllt house
would easily bring the total capital of the
combination up to the billion dollar mark,
and perhaps even to a billion and a half
dollars.
â¢'Railroad men and financiers to-day bo
lieve that the Rockefellers are going to
assume tho control and direction of the i
Vanderbilt railroad Interests.
"In the West the effect of Rockefeller ]
control is likely to be felt in tho pushing ;
: of Mr. Vanderbilt's schema of a groat
' transcontinental lino."
The. World says: "The report that a
; billion dollar railroad combine has been
, formed between Vnnderbilt and the
j Rockefellers ip without any foundation
, whatever. The election of William Rocke
feller as director of the New -York Central
â¢ road means nothing whatever except an
I investment.
"It Is quite probable that William T\.
1 Vanderbilt may at an early day realize
â  the ambition of his family and own a
i transcontinental line connecting the
waters of the Atlantic and Pacific, but
the report that ho has secured control of
the Southern Pacific road through Bp#yer
&' Co. la not true. Speyer & Co. are Ilunt
lngton brokers and would hardly carry
out Mr. Vanderbik's contrary instruc
tions."
TEACHERS WILL NOT
LOSE THEIR PLACES
SCHOOL BOAKD'S MISTAKE TO
BE RECTIFIED.
Through tho faulty verbiage of a reso
lution adopted by the Board of Education
at its last meeting twelv^ pedagogues
| lost not only their positions and per
! Quisites as "heads of departments" hut as
j teachers in the school department as
â¢well. It was not the intention of the Di
[ rectors to throw them out of the depart
i ment; the one object was to abolish the
j position of "departmental head" and save
to the depleted school fund the $15 per
month -which attaches to positions of that
classification.
As soon as the error was discovered a
meeting of the twelve heads involved -was
called and thoy came together at Super
intendent Webster's office at the City
Hall. The meeting had a double-barreled
object; to learn Just what was their
status in the department and to fight for
i the recreation of their old positions.
Superintendent Webster was called in and
upon the tlrst point was quick to reassure
them. He said that if through faulty
construction it was made to appear in the
resolution that they had bt-en dropped
from the department the mistake would
be rectified. Upon the second point he
held out no assurance.
The Board of Education, he explained,
considered that the position of head of
department had come to be little more
than honorary as there were no dis
tinctive responsibilities or duties attached
to It. This he expressed as his opinion,
but said he -would advise the discharged
heads to get together as a body and
formulate a certain line of duties other
than their regular obligations as teachers
In the department and then memorialize
the board to re-establish the positions.
The teachers did not agree with their
superintendent that they had not already
distinctive duties and responsibilities.
They argued that they were responsible
not only to the School Department of San
Francisco but as well to the faculty of
the State University. The departments
of which they -were the heads were all ac
credited by the State University and it
was obligatory upon them to see to it
that the teachers In the departments of
â which they had supervision brotight their
classes up to the standard set by the
State institution. They claimed that this
responsibility alone was worth the $15
per month -which -was added to their
monthly stipend.
They could not argue profitably, how
ever, in face of the opinion of the Board
of Education and finally decided to fol
low the advice of their superintendent
and memorialize the board for the re
establishment of the position and the
repayment of the $15 per month.

FIRE IN WESTERN ADDITION.
The O^Kane Residence Narrowly Es
capes Total Destruction.
The residence of W. D. O'Kane at 2119
Broadway had a narrow escape from total
destruction by lire yesterday afternoon,
shortly after 2 o'clock. The flames orig
inated, on the roof and were caused by a
spark from a chimney. Before the fire
could be subdued the entire top of the
structure had been destroyed, and tho
damage will amount to several thousand
dollars.
As soon as the fire was discovered an
alarm was turned in from box 25fi at Fill
more street and Pacific avenue. The Fire
Department was quick in responding:, but
the blaze had already gained considerable
headway and much difficulty was experi
enced in 'extinguishing it. as the fire was
confined between the roof and the ceiling
of the upper story. Much damage was
done tt> the interior of the structure by
water and articles of considerable value
wore ruined. W. D. O'Kane, owner of the
house, is one of the proprietors of the
"Union Transfer Company in this city.
To Be Sent Back to Texas.
The Tarantino family at 51 Jessie street,
consisting of a mother and three
children, all of whom are weak-minded
and whose deplorable condition was de
tailed in Tuesday's Call, will be shipped
back to Texas, whence they came. Mayor
Phelan is assisting the California Society
for the Protection of Children to that de
sirable end. <
SUIT FOR POSSESSION
OF MISSION ROCK
TJNTTED STATES WANTS IT FOB
A COALING STATION.
United States Attorney Coombs yester
day prepared a complaint to be filed to
day in the United States Circuit Court
asking for th<j ejectment of the California
Drydock Company from Mission Rock, in
San Francisco Bay. The United States
Government wants the rock to be used as
a coaling station. The complaint recites
that the patent issued by the State of
California In 1870 is valid only as to what
ever lands may have been at that time
under the sovereignty of the State, but
contends that no State patent can hold as
against the United States in the case of
any Islands or other realty over which
the United States has sovereignty.
' Mr. Coombs said yesterday that it had
been held by the courts that the United
States is entitled to the islands in the
bay of San Francisco and to all the tide
lands in the State and lands that
are covered and uncovered by the
ebb and flow of tides reaching
these lands at ordinary high tides. All
lands below ordinary high tides belong to
the State.
There will be two contests in the trial
of the case â one as to what the area of
Mission Rock was at the time it was
filled in and whether it consisted of a
Jutting rock or whether the lands about
it were above ordinary high-water mark.
Mr. Coombs will also present this ques
tion, as to whether tide and submerged
lands, the title to which is vested in the
Stat< j by reason of its sovereignty, can
pertain to those tide lands which sur
round islands where the State has no
sovereignty.
The latter is a new point, and its treat
ment by the court and counsel will be of
much interest.
Does he drink? No; but he usps
regularly The Old Government "Whisky.
It stimulates, tones, satisfies, but does
not act like a drug nor create an ab
normal craving. â¢
ACCUSED THEM FALSELY.
A Colored Sailor Brutally Beats a
Japanese and His Wife.
George Allen, a colored sailor on a
coasting vessel, while drunk last night
had a racket in an Oneida-place house,
which caused a charge of assault with a
deadly weapon and one of battery to be
placed against his name at the California
street station. Shortly after Allen had
entered the alley he claimed that he had
been robbed of $45 by a Japanese woman.
He promptly beat her husband, S. Aru
nura on the head with a chair and as
saulted the woman with his fists. Aru
nura suffered considerably from his
wounds and the woman was badly In
jured. Officers Dinan and "Williams "final
ly arrested the sailor after a desperate
struggle. When searched at the police
station Allen still had the $45 in his pos- i
session.
